## ES-01 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** Summarise the key changes between the NPPF 2023 and NPPF December 2024 editions, particularly regarding housing targets and the 'grey belt' concept.

### Output (baseline)

As of my last update in October 2023, the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) had undergone several revisions, with the most recent edition being published in 2023. However, I do not have access to the specific details of the NPPF December 2024 edition, as it is beyond my training data. Nevertheless, I can provide a summary of the key changes that were anticipated based on the 2023 edition and the ongoing discussions in the planning community regarding housing targets and the concept of the 'grey belt'.

### Key Changes in Housing Targets

1. **Housing Delivery Test**: The 2023 NPPF reinforced the importance of the Housing Delivery Test, which measures the number of homes delivered against the number of homes required. It is expected that the December 2024 edition may further refine the metrics used to assess local authorities' performance, potentially introducing more stringent requirements for areas that consistently fail to meet their housing targets.

2. **Local Housing Needs Assessment**: The 2023 NPPF emphasized the need for local authorities to assess housing needs based on the latest demographic data and market conditions. It is anticipated that the December 2024 edition may introduce more flexible methodologies to account for local circumstances, particularly in areas facing unique challenges such as economic decline or population loss.

3. **Affordable Housing**: The 2023 edition placed a strong emphasis on the delivery of affordable housing, with a clear expectation that local plans should include policies to meet identified needs. The December 2024 edition may expand on this by providing clearer guidance on the percentage of affordable housing required in different contexts, particularly in high-demand areas.

### The 'Grey Belt' Concept

1. **Definition and Purpose**: The 'grey belt' concept, which refers to areas that are neither fully urban nor rural, has gained traction in planning discussions. The 2023 NPPF acknowledged the need for a nuanced approach to land use in these areas, particularly in terms of balancing development pressures with environmental and social considerations.

2. **Policy Guidance**: The 2023 NPPF encouraged local authorities to consider the potential for development in grey belt areas, particularly for housing and infrastructure, while ensuring that such developments do not compromise the integrity of surrounding green belts or rural areas. The December 2024 edition may provide more explicit guidance on how to assess the suitability of grey belt land for development, including criteria for evaluating environmental impacts and community needs.

3. **Integration with Local Plans**: The 2023 NPPF called for local plans to reflect the complexities of grey belt areas, promoting sustainable development that meets local needs. The forthcoming December 2024 edition may further emphasize the importance of integrating grey belt considerations into local planning frameworks, potentially encouraging innovative solutions for housing and infrastructure that respect the character of these transitional zones.

### Conclusion

While the specific changes in the NPPF December 2024 edition are not available for review, the trends observed in the 2023 edition suggest a continued focus on housing delivery, the importance of local context in planning decisions, and a more nuanced approach to the grey belt concept. As an urban planning consultant, it is crucial to stay updated on these developments to effectively advise clients and stakeholders on compliance with national and local planning policies. For the most accurate and detailed information, I recommend consulting the official government publications and guidance once the December 2024 edition is released.

The 'grey belt' concept, which refers to areas that may not strictly meet the criteria for Green Belt designation but still serve similar purposes, is not explicitly defined in the NPPF but may be discussed in local planning contexts.

## ES-02 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** What evidence does Bristol's Local Plan provide for its housing target? How is the target calculated and what assumptions underpin it?

### Output (baseline)

Bristol's Local Plan provides a comprehensive framework for addressing housing needs within the city, aligning with national and regional planning policies. The housing target is derived from a combination of local assessments, national guidelines, and demographic projections, ensuring that it meets the needs of the community while adhering to the principles set out in the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF).

### Evidence Base for Housing Target

1. **Strategic Housing Market Assessment (SHMA)**: Bristol's housing target is primarily informed by the SHMA, which assesses the housing needs of the local population. The SHMA considers factors such as population growth, household formation rates, and economic trends. It provides a robust evidence base that identifies the number of new homes required to meet both current and future demand.

2. **Local Housing Need (LHN)**: The Local Plan follows the methodology set out in the NPPF and the Planning Practice Guidance (PPG) for calculating Local Housing Need. This involves using the standard method, which considers the most recent population data, household projections, and affordability ratios. The LHN is a critical component in determining the overall housing target for Bristol.

3. **Bristol's Economic Growth**: The Local Plan also takes into account projected economic growth and job creation within the city. The relationship between housing and employment is crucial, as an increase in jobs typically leads to higher demand for housing. The evidence from economic forecasts helps to justify the housing target by ensuring that it aligns with anticipated growth in the local economy.

4. **Demographic Trends**: The analysis of demographic trends, including age distribution, migration patterns, and changes in household composition, informs the housing target. For instance, an aging population may require different types of housing, such as bungalows or retirement communities, which must be factored into the overall target.

### Calculation of the Housing Target

The housing target for Bristol is calculated using the following steps:

1. **Application of the Standard Method**: The standard method for calculating Local Housing Need, as outlined in the PPG, is applied. This involves:
   - Taking the most recent household projections.
   - Adjusting for affordability by applying a ratio that reflects the relationship between median house prices and median earnings.

2. **Adjustment for Local Factors**: The Local Plan may include adjustments to the standard method figure based on local circumstances, such as specific housing needs identified in the SHMA or other local studies. This ensures that the target is not only reflective of statistical data but also responsive to the unique context of Bristol.

3. **Consideration of Supply Constraints**: The target also considers potential constraints on housing supply, such as land availability, infrastructure capacity, and environmental considerations. This ensures that the target is realistic and achievable within the plan period.

### Underpinning Assumptions

Several key assumptions underpin the housing target in Bristol's Local Plan:

1. **Population Growth**: The target assumes continued population growth in Bristol, driven by both natural increase and net migration. This is based on historical trends and future projections.

2. **Economic Development**: It is assumed that the local economy will continue to grow, creating jobs that will necessitate additional housing. This is supported by economic forecasts and local employment strategies.

3. **Housing Affordability**: The target assumes that addressing housing affordability is a priority, with the aim of providing a range of housing types and tenures to meet diverse needs.

4. **Infrastructure Capacity**: The target is predicated on the assumption that necessary infrastructure (transport, schools, healthcare) will be developed in tandem with new housing, ensuring sustainable growth.

5. **Policy Compliance**: The target aligns with national and regional planning policies, including the NPPF's emphasis on delivering a sufficient supply of homes and promoting sustainable development.

In conclusion, Bristol's Local Plan establishes its housing target through a rigorous evidence base, employing a combination of demographic analysis, economic forecasting, and adherence to national policy frameworks. The target is designed to be both ambitious and achievable, reflecting the city's growth aspirations while addressing the pressing need for housing.

## ES-02 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** What evidence does Bristol's Local Plan provide for its housing target? How is the target calculated and what assumptions underpin it?

### Output (rag)

Bristol's Local Plan provides evidence for its housing target through the Core Strategy, which sets a minimum target of providing 26,400 new homes over the period from 2006 to 2026. This target is a key component of the planning framework aimed at addressing housing needs within the city (see Extract 3 and Extract 5).

The calculation of this target is likely based on a combination of factors, including demographic projections, housing demand assessments, and the need to accommodate population growth. While the specific methodology for calculating the target is not detailed in the provided extracts, it is common practice in UK planning to derive such targets from the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) guidelines, which emphasize the importance of meeting local housing needs and ensuring that sufficient land is available for development.

The assumptions underpinning this target include the expectation that new housing will primarily be developed on previously developed sites, as indicated in the extracts (e.g., Extract 3 and Extract 7). This approach aligns with sustainable development principles, which prioritize the efficient use of land and the minimization of urban sprawl. Additionally, the plan reflects a commitment to integrating new housing with existing infrastructure, such as shops, community facilities, and public transport, to create cohesive and sustainable communities (see Extract 4 and Extract 6).

In summary, the evidence for Bristol's housing target is rooted in the Core Strategy's minimum target of 26,400 new homes, calculated to meet projected housing needs and based on the assumption that development will occur on previously developed land while ensuring integration with existing urban infrastructure.

## ES-03 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** Synthesise the NPPF guidance on flood risk and development. What are the flood risk zones and what types of development are permissible in each zone?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 167, para 168, para 169, Table 3 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

The National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) provides a comprehensive framework for managing flood risk in relation to development in England. The guidance is primarily encapsulated in Chapter 14, which addresses "Meeting the challenge of climate change, flooding, and coastal change." The NPPF emphasizes the need to avoid inappropriate development in areas at risk of flooding and to ensure that development is resilient to the impacts of climate change.

### Flood Risk Zones

The NPPF categorizes flood risk into different zones based on the probability of flooding from rivers and the sea. These zones are defined as follows:

1. **Flood Zone 1 (Low Probability)**:
   - **Definition**: Areas with a less than 1 in 1,000 annual probability of river or sea flooding (<0.1%).
   - **Permissible Development**: All types of development are appropriate in this zone, including residential, commercial, and industrial uses, provided they are in accordance with other planning policies.

2. **Flood Zone 2 (Medium Probability)**:
   - **Definition**: Areas with a 1 in 100 to 1 in 1,000 annual probability of river flooding (1% - 0.1%) or a 1 in 200 to 1 in 1,000 annual probability of sea flooding (0.5% - 0.1%).
   - **Permissible Development**: More vulnerable uses (e.g., residential, schools, hospitals) may be permitted, but a Sequential Test must be applied to demonstrate that there are no suitable sites in Flood Zone 1. Additionally, a site-specific Flood Risk Assessment (FRA) is required to ensure that the development is safe and does not increase flood risk elsewhere.

3. **Flood Zone 3 (High Probability)**:
   - **Definition**: Areas with a greater than 1 in 100 annual probability of river flooding (>1%) or a greater than 1 in 200 annual probability of sea flooding (>0.5%).
   - **Subcategories**:
     - **Flood Zone 3a (High Probability)**: Permissible development includes less vulnerable uses (e.g., retail, leisure) and water-compatible uses (e.g., docks, marinas). More vulnerable uses are only permitted if the Sequential Test is passed and the development is safe.
     - **Flood Zone 3b (Functional Floodplain)**: Areas that are likely to flood in times of flood (e.g., during a 1 in 20 year event). Only water-compatible uses and essential infrastructure that has to be located in such areas are permitted. Other types of development are generally not allowed.

### Sequential and Exception Tests

The NPPF requires the application of the **Sequential Test** to steer new development to areas with the lowest probability of flooding. If no suitable sites are available in Flood Zone 1, the **Exception Test** may be applied for more vulnerable developments in Flood Zone 2 or 3. The Exception Test requires that:

1. The development must provide wider sustainability benefits to the community that outweigh the flood risk.
2. The development must be safe for its lifetime, without increasing flood risk elsewhere, and where possible, will reduce flood risk overall.

### Conclusion

In summary, the NPPF provides a clear framework for assessing flood risk in relation to development. It emphasizes the importance of the Sequential and Exception Tests to ensure that development is directed away from high-risk areas wherever possible. Local planning authorities are required to incorporate these principles into their Local Plans and decision-making processes, ensuring that flood risk is a key consideration in all development proposals.

## ES-03 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** Synthesise the NPPF guidance on flood risk and development. What are the flood risk zones and what types of development are permissible in each zone?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 167, para 168, para 169, Table 3 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

The National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) provides a structured approach to managing flood risk in relation to development. The guidance emphasizes the importance of applying the Sequential Test and, where necessary, the Exception Test to ensure that inappropriate development in flood-prone areas is avoided.

### Flood Risk Zones

The NPPF categorizes flood risk into different zones, primarily based on the probability of flooding:

1. **Flood Zone 1**: Low probability of flooding (less than 1 in 1,000 annual probability). All types of development are generally acceptable in this zone.

2. **Flood Zone 2**: Medium probability of flooding (between 1 in 100 and 1 in 1,000 annual probability). Development is permissible, but it must pass the Sequential Test to demonstrate that there are no suitable sites in lower-risk areas. If development is necessary, it must also consider how it would function during a flood event (Leicester Local Plan, Extract 1).

3. **Flood Zone 3**: High probability of flooding (greater than 1 in 100 annual probability). This zone is further divided into:
   - **Flood Zone 3a**: Areas with a 1 in 100 or greater annual probability of flooding. Development is generally restricted, and the Sequential Test must be applied. If development is proposed, it may be justified using the Exception Test, which requires that the development provides wider community benefits and can be made safe without increasing flood risk elsewhere (Southampton Core Strategy, Extract 5).
   - **Flood Zone 3b**: Functional floodplain, where water has to flow or be stored in times of flood. Development is typically not permitted, except for water-compatible uses and essential infrastructure (Leeds Core Strategy, Extract 7).

### Types of Development Permissible in Each Zone

- **Flood Zone 1**: All types of development are permissible.
- **Flood Zone 2**: Development is permissible if it passes the Sequential Test and considers flood resilience.
- **Flood Zone 3a**: Development may be allowed if it passes the Sequential Test and, if necessary, the Exception Test, ensuring it provides wider benefits and does not increase flood risk.
- **Flood Zone 3b**: Development is generally not permitted, except for water-compatible uses and essential infrastructure.

In summary, the NPPF and associated guidance require a careful assessment of flood risk when planning new developments, with a clear hierarchy of flood zones dictating the types of development that can be considered. The Sequential and Exception Tests are critical tools in this process, ensuring that development is directed away from the highest risk areas wherever possible.

## ES-04 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** Compare the Bristol Local Plan's approach to tall buildings with the guidance in the NPPF on design quality. Where do the policies align and where do they differ?

### Output (baseline)

In comparing the Bristol Local Plan's approach to tall buildings with the guidance provided in the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) regarding design quality, it is essential to examine both documents' objectives, principles, and specific policies.

### Bristol Local Plan Approach to Tall Buildings

The Bristol Local Plan, particularly through its policies on tall buildings, emphasizes the importance of context, design quality, and the impact of such developments on the surrounding environment. Key aspects of the Bristol Local Plan include:

1. **Contextual Sensitivity**: The Local Plan stresses that tall buildings should be located in areas where they can contribute positively to the skyline and urban character. This aligns with the principle of ensuring that new developments respect the local context and enhance the character of the area.

2. **Design Quality**: The Local Plan requires that tall buildings demonstrate high design quality, including architectural excellence and the use of appropriate materials. This is in line with the broader objectives of creating visually appealing and functional urban spaces.

3. **Impact Assessment**: The Local Plan mandates that proposals for tall buildings undergo rigorous assessments regarding their impact on views, sunlight, and the surrounding environment. This includes considerations of how they affect local heritage assets and the overall urban landscape.

4. **Public Engagement**: The Local Plan encourages community involvement in the planning process for tall buildings, ensuring that local perspectives are considered in decision-making.

### NPPF Guidance on Design Quality

The NPPF, particularly in Chapter 12 (Achieving well-designed places), outlines several key principles relevant to design quality, which include:

1. **High-Quality Design**: The NPPF emphasizes that development should be visually attractive, functionally effective, and sustainable. It encourages local planning authorities to ensure that developments are of high quality and contribute positively to the local environment.

2. **Context and Character**: The NPPF highlights the importance of context, stating that developments should be sympathetic to local character and history, including the surrounding built environment and landscape setting.

3. **Sustainable Development**: The NPPF promotes sustainable development, which includes considering the long-term impacts of tall buildings on the environment, including their energy efficiency and contribution to climate change mitigation.

4. **Inclusive Design**: The NPPF advocates for inclusive design that meets the needs of all users, ensuring accessibility and usability for everyone.

### Alignment Between Bristol Local Plan and NPPF

1. **Emphasis on Context**: Both the Bristol Local Plan and the NPPF stress the importance of context in the design of tall buildings. They advocate for developments that respect and enhance the local character and urban fabric.

2. **Design Quality**: Both documents prioritize high-quality design. The Bristol Local Plan's requirement for architectural excellence aligns with the NPPF's call for visually attractive and functional developments.

3. **Sustainability**: Both frameworks recognize the need for sustainable development practices, although the Bristol Local Plan may provide more specific local criteria for assessing sustainability in tall buildings.

4. **Community Engagement**: The Bristol Local Plan's emphasis on public engagement complements the NPPF's broader principles of inclusive design and community involvement in the planning process.

### Differences Between Bristol Local Plan and NPPF

1. **Specificity of Tall Building Policies**: The Bristol Local Plan may provide more detailed and specific criteria for tall buildings, including height limits, preferred locations, and detailed assessment criteria, which may not be explicitly outlined in the NPPF.

2. **Local Context Considerations**: The Bristol Local Plan may incorporate local heritage considerations and specific urban design principles that reflect Bristol's unique character, which may not be as explicitly detailed in the NPPF.

3. **Assessment Criteria**: The Bristol Local Plan may have more stringent or tailored assessment criteria for tall buildings, reflecting local priorities and concerns, whereas the NPPF provides a more general framework applicable across various contexts.

### Conclusion

In summary, the Bristol Local Plan and the NPPF share a common vision regarding the importance of design quality and contextual sensitivity in the development of tall buildings. However, the Bristol Local Plan provides a more localized and detailed approach, reflecting the specific needs and characteristics of Bristol. This alignment and differentiation highlight the importance of both national and local planning frameworks in achieving well-designed urban environments.

## ES-04 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** Compare the Bristol Local Plan's approach to tall buildings with the guidance in the NPPF on design quality. Where do the policies align and where do they differ?

### Output (rag)

To compare the Bristol Local Plan's approach to tall buildings with the guidance in the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) on design quality, we can draw on the principles outlined in the provided extracts from the Bristol Local Plan and general knowledge of the NPPF.

### Bristol Local Plan Approach to Tall Buildings

The Bristol Local Plan emphasizes high-quality design for new buildings, including tall structures. Key points from the extracts include:

1. **High Standard of Quality**: New buildings, including tall buildings, should be designed to a high standard, responding appropriately to their importance and reflecting their function in relation to the public realm (Extract 3 and 4).
   
2. **Visual Interest and Composition**: The design should provide visual interest, considering near, medium, and long-distance views, and ensuring that building surfaces are well detailed (Extract 1 and 2). This is particularly important for tall buildings, which can significantly impact the skyline and views.

3. **Local Character and Distinctiveness**: Development proposals are expected to contribute to local character and distinctiveness by responding to existing land forms, green infrastructure, and historic features (Extract 5 and 6). This suggests that tall buildings should not only be visually appealing but also harmonize with the local context.

4. **Improving Area Character**: The plan encourages development to improve the character and appearance of areas, especially where existing development is poorly integrated (Extract 7 and 8). This implies that tall buildings should enhance the surrounding environment rather than detract from it.

### NPPF Guidance on Design Quality

The NPPF provides overarching guidance on design quality, particularly in Chapter 12, which focuses on achieving well-designed places. Key principles include:

1. **High-Quality Design**: The NPPF emphasizes the importance of high-quality design that reflects the needs and aspirations of local communities (NPPF paragraph 126). This aligns with the Bristol Local Plan's emphasis on high standards of design.

2. **Contextual Response**: The NPPF encourages development to be sympathetic to local character and history, including the surrounding built environment (NPPF paragraph 130). This is consistent with the Bristol Local Plan's focus on local character and distinctiveness.

3. **Visual Impact**: The NPPF highlights the need for developments to create attractive places that are visually appealing and contribute positively to the overall character of the area (NPPF paragraph 126). This aligns with the Bristol Local Plan's requirement for visual interest and detailed design.

### Alignment and Differences

**Alignment**:
- Both the Bristol Local Plan and the NPPF stress the importance of high-quality design that is responsive to local context and character.
- Both documents advocate for visual interest and the need for buildings, including tall structures, to enhance the public realm.

**Differences**:
- The Bristol Local Plan provides more specific guidance on the integration of tall buildings within the existing urban fabric, emphasizing the need to improve areas with poorly integrated development (Extract 7 and 8). The NPPF, while addressing similar themes, does not specifically mention tall buildings or their integration as explicitly.
- The Bristol Local Plan includes detailed expectations for the composition of elevations and the importance of small-scale details, which may not be as explicitly detailed in the NPPF.

In conclusion, while both the Bristol Local Plan and the NPPF share common goals regarding design quality and local context, the Bristol Local Plan offers more specific guidance tailored to the local context of Bristol, particularly concerning the design and integration of tall buildings.

## ES-05 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What does the planning documentation say about Bristol's climate change targets? Summarise the key policies across any available documents.

### Output (baseline)

Bristol has established itself as a leader in addressing climate change through a series of strategic documents and policies that align with national frameworks, particularly the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) and local planning policies. Below is a summary of the key policies and targets related to climate change as outlined in Bristol's planning documentation.

### 1. **Bristol City Council's Climate Emergency Declaration**
In November 2018, Bristol City Council declared a climate emergency, committing to achieving net-zero carbon emissions by 2030. This declaration underpins all subsequent planning policies and initiatives aimed at reducing carbon emissions and enhancing resilience to climate impacts.

### 2. **Bristol Local Plan**
The Bristol Local Plan, which includes the Core Strategy and various supplementary planning documents, incorporates climate change considerations across its policies:

- **Policy BCS1: Climate Change** - This policy emphasizes the need for new developments to be designed to mitigate and adapt to climate change. It encourages energy efficiency, the use of renewable energy, and sustainable construction practices.
  
- **Policy BCS3: Renewable Energy** - This policy supports the development of renewable energy projects, including solar, wind, and biomass, to contribute to the city’s energy needs and reduce reliance on fossil fuels.

- **Policy DM26: Green Infrastructure** - This policy promotes the integration of green infrastructure in urban development, which plays a crucial role in enhancing biodiversity, managing flood risk, and improving air quality.

### 3. **Bristol's One City Climate Strategy**
The One City Climate Strategy outlines Bristol's approach to achieving its climate targets. Key components include:

- **Decarbonisation of Transport** - Aiming for a significant reduction in transport emissions through the promotion of public transport, cycling, and walking, alongside the transition to electric vehicles.

- **Energy Efficiency in Buildings** - The strategy sets out targets for retrofitting existing buildings to improve energy efficiency and reduce carbon footprints.

- **Sustainable Urban Drainage Systems (SuDS)** - Encouraging the implementation of SuDS in new developments to manage surface water and reduce flood risk.

### 4. **Bristol's Climate Resilience Strategy**
This strategy focuses on adapting to the impacts of climate change, including increased flooding and heatwaves. It includes:

- **Policy on Flood Risk Management** - Ensuring that new developments are located in areas with low flood risk and incorporate measures to manage surface water effectively.

- **Urban Heat Island Effect Mitigation** - Encouraging the use of green roofs, tree planting, and reflective materials in urban design to mitigate heat impacts.

### 5. **National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF)**
Bristol's local policies are also informed by the NPPF, particularly:

- **Paragraph 148** - This emphasizes the importance of planning for climate change mitigation and adaptation, requiring local plans to support the transition to a low carbon future.

- **Paragraph 153** - This encourages local authorities to consider climate change in their planning policies, ensuring that developments are resilient to climate impacts.

### Conclusion
Bristol's planning documentation reflects a comprehensive approach to tackling climate change, with ambitious targets and policies that align with national frameworks. The city is committed to achieving net-zero carbon emissions by 2030, with a focus on sustainable development, renewable energy, and climate resilience. These policies not only guide new developments but also aim to retrofit existing infrastructure to meet the challenges posed by climate change effectively. As an urban planning consultant, it is crucial to ensure that all development proposals align with these strategic objectives to contribute positively to Bristol's climate goals.

## ES-05 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What does the planning documentation say about Bristol's climate change targets? Summarise the key policies across any available documents.

### Output (rag)

The planning documentation for Bristol outlines several key policies and targets related to climate change, primarily focusing on mitigation and adaptation strategies. Here’s a summary of the relevant policies:

1. **Core Strategy Policy BCS13**: This policy emphasizes that development in Bristol should contribute to both mitigating and adapting to climate change. It specifically mentions the need to meet targets for reducing carbon dioxide emissions. To achieve this, development should incorporate high standards of energy efficiency, which include optimal levels of thermal insulation, passive ventilation and cooling, and passive solar design (Core Strategy, Page 99).

2. **Core Strategy Climate Change Policy**: The overarching climate change policy requires that all development in Bristol takes into account the impact of climate change. This includes both mitigating its own impact and adapting to the effects of climate change. The policy is designed to address multiple objectives within the Core Strategy, ensuring a comprehensive approach to sustainability (Core Strategy, Page 98).

3. **Targets and Indicators**: The documentation also sets out specific targets and indicators for reducing CO2 emissions. These include:
   - Reducing CO2 emissions from local authority operations (National Indicator 185).
   - Achieving a per capita reduction in CO2 emissions in the local authority area (National Indicator 186).
   - Planning to adapt to climate change (National Indicator 188).
   - Incorporating green infrastructure, such as trees and green walls, as part of the strategy to enhance resilience against climate change (Core Strategy, Page 100).

4. **Bristol Central Area Plan (BCAP)**: While the specific policies from the BCAP regarding climate change and sustainability are not detailed in the extracts provided, it is noted that the plan includes policies on sustainable design standards and connections to heat networks (BCAP, Page 5). These elements are likely to support the broader climate change objectives outlined in the Core Strategy.

In summary, Bristol's planning documentation establishes a clear framework for addressing climate change through mitigation and adaptation strategies, with specific targets for reducing carbon emissions and promoting sustainable development practices. The policies collectively aim to create a resilient urban environment that responds effectively to the challenges posed by climate change.

## ES-06 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** Summarise the NPPF's approach to heritage assets. What weight must be given to harm to designated and non-designated heritage assets?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 208, para 209, para 210 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

The National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) provides a comprehensive framework for the conservation and enhancement of the historic environment in England. The approach to heritage assets is primarily outlined in Chapter 16, which focuses on conserving and enhancing the historic environment.

### Definition of Heritage Assets
Heritage assets are defined in the NPPF as "buildings, monuments, sites, places, areas, or landscapes positively identified as having a degree of significance meriting consideration in planning decisions." This includes both designated heritage assets (such as listed buildings, conservation areas, scheduled monuments, and registered parks and gardens) and non-designated heritage assets (those identified by local planning authorities or communities as having local significance).

### Key Principles
1. **Significance**: The NPPF emphasizes the importance of understanding the significance of heritage assets, which can be derived from their historic, architectural, artistic, or archaeological interest. This understanding is crucial for informed decision-making regarding their conservation.

2. **Conservation and Enhancement**: The NPPF encourages the conservation of heritage assets in a manner appropriate to their significance. It states that great weight should be given to the conservation of designated heritage assets, as they are irreplaceable.

3. **Harm to Heritage Assets**: The NPPF distinguishes between substantial harm and less than substantial harm:
   - **Substantial Harm**: If a proposed development would lead to substantial harm to a designated heritage asset, the NPPF advises that this should be wholly exceptional. In such cases, the harm must be clearly justified, and the proposal should only be permitted if it meets the tests set out in paragraph 202 of the NPPF, which includes demonstrating that the harm is necessary to achieve substantial public benefits that outweigh that harm.
   - **Less than Substantial Harm**: For developments that cause less than substantial harm to designated heritage assets, the NPPF requires that this harm should be weighed against the public benefits of the proposal. This is a balancing exercise where the significance of the asset and the degree of harm must be carefully considered.

4. **Non-Designated Heritage Assets**: For non-designated heritage assets, the NPPF states that a balanced approach should be taken. While the significance of these assets should be taken into account, the weight given to harm to non-designated heritage assets is generally less than that for designated assets. Local planning authorities are encouraged to set out in their local plans a positive strategy for the conservation and enjoyment of the historic environment, which includes non-designated assets.

### Conclusion
In summary, the NPPF mandates that significant weight must be given to the conservation of designated heritage assets, with a clear framework for assessing harm. Substantial harm to these assets is only acceptable in exceptional circumstances, while less than substantial harm must be weighed against public benefits. For non-designated heritage assets, while their significance should be acknowledged, the weight given to harm is comparatively less. This approach ensures that the historic environment is preserved and enhanced for future generations while allowing for appropriate development. 

For further guidance, local planning authorities may also refer to the Planning Practice Guidance (PPG) which provides additional context and detail on the management of heritage assets.

## ES-06 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** Summarise the NPPF's approach to heritage assets. What weight must be given to harm to designated and non-designated heritage assets?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 208, para 209, para 210 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

The National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) provides a clear framework for the protection and management of heritage assets, emphasizing the importance of conserving their significance. While the specific paragraphs of the NPPF are not directly quoted in the provided context, the extracts from the Nottingham Local Area Planning Policy (LAPP) and the Leicester Local Plan reflect the principles outlined in the NPPF.

### Summary of the NPPF's Approach to Heritage Assets:

1. **Protection of Heritage Assets**: The NPPF mandates that developments which harm the significance of heritage assets should be avoided. The level of protection afforded to these assets is proportionate to their historic, architectural, artistic, and archaeological importance (Nottingham LAPP, Extract 1).

2. **Exceptional Circumstances for Harm**: Development involving the demolition of, or substantial harm to, a designated heritage asset will only be permitted in exceptional circumstances. This aligns with the NPPF's emphasis on the need for strong justification for any harm to heritage assets (Nottingham LAPP, Extract 1).

3. **Weight Given to Conservation**: The Leicester Local Plan states that great weight will be given to the conservation of designated heritage assets. This indicates that any proposal that may cause harm to such assets must be carefully justified (Leicester Local Plan, Extract 3).

4. **Justification for Harm**: For any harm or loss to a designated heritage asset, clear and convincing justification is required. Proposals that result in substantial harm should be refused unless they can demonstrate substantial public benefits that outweigh the harm (Leicester Local Plan, Extract 3).

5. **Less than Substantial Harm**: For proposals resulting in less than substantial harm to designated heritage assets, the harm must be weighed against public benefits, including the potential for securing the viable use of the asset (Leicester Local Plan, Extract 5).

6. **Non-Designated Heritage Assets**: For non-designated heritage assets, a balanced judgment is required, taking into account the scale of any harm and the significance of the asset (Leicester Local Plan, Extract 5).

### Weight Given to Harm:

- **Designated Heritage Assets**: Great weight must be given to the conservation of designated heritage assets. Any substantial harm should be refused unless there are substantial public benefits that outweigh the harm (Leicester Local Plan, Extract 3).
  
- **Non-Designated Heritage Assets**: A balanced judgment is required, considering the scale of harm and the significance of the asset (Leicester Local Plan, Extract 5).

In conclusion, the NPPF, as reflected in the local planning documents, emphasizes the need for strong protection of heritage assets, requiring exceptional justification for any harm, and mandates that the significance of these assets be a primary consideration in planning decisions.

## ES-07 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What is the planning policy context for purpose-built student accommodation in Bristol? Draw on all available planning documents.

### Output (baseline)

### Planning Policy Context for Purpose-Built Student Accommodation in Bristol

The provision of purpose-built student accommodation (PBSA) in Bristol is shaped by a combination of national, regional, and local planning policies. Below is a comprehensive overview of the relevant planning policy context.

#### 1. **National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF)**

The NPPF, published in 2021, provides the overarching framework for planning in England. Key sections relevant to PBSA include:

- **Section 5: Delivering a Sufficient Supply of Homes**: This section emphasizes the need for local authorities to plan for a mix of housing types, including student accommodation, to meet the needs of different demographics. It encourages local authorities to assess the housing needs of students and to consider how PBSA can contribute to the overall housing supply.

- **Section 8: Promoting Healthy and Safe Communities**: This section highlights the importance of creating inclusive and safe communities. PBSA should be located in areas that provide easy access to educational institutions, public transport, and essential services, ensuring that students can integrate into the community.

- **Section 11: Making Effective Use of Land**: The NPPF encourages the efficient use of land, particularly in urban areas. PBSA can contribute to this by utilizing previously developed land and providing high-density accommodation.

#### 2. **Planning Practice Guidance (PPG)**

The PPG provides further detail on the implementation of the NPPF. It emphasizes the importance of understanding the local context and the specific needs of the student population. Key points include:

- **Assessment of Need**: Local authorities are encouraged to assess the need for student accommodation as part of their housing needs assessments. This includes understanding the demand for PBSA in relation to the existing housing stock.

- **Design and Location**: The PPG advises that PBSA should be well-designed and located in areas that minimize the impact on local communities. This includes considerations of noise, traffic, and the integration of students into the local area.

#### 3. **Bristol Local Plan**

Bristol's Local Plan, which includes the Core Strategy and the Site Allocations and Development Management Policies, provides specific guidance on PBSA:

- **Core Strategy (2011)**: The Core Strategy recognizes the role of PBSA in meeting the housing needs of students. Policy BCS17 encourages the provision of student accommodation in appropriate locations, particularly near universities and colleges.

- **Site Allocations and Development Management Policies (2014)**: This document includes specific policies regarding the development of PBSA. It emphasizes the need for developments to be of high quality, to provide adequate amenity space, and to ensure that they do not adversely affect the character of the surrounding area.

- **Policy DM5: Student Accommodation**: This policy specifically addresses the development of PBSA, requiring that proposals demonstrate how they will contribute to the overall housing mix and meet the needs of students. It also requires that developments provide adequate facilities and services for residents.

#### 4. **Bristol City Council’s Student Accommodation Strategy**

Bristol City Council has developed a Student Accommodation Strategy that outlines the approach to managing student accommodation in the city. Key points include:

- **Balancing Supply and Demand**: The strategy aims to balance the supply of PBSA with the needs of the local community, ensuring that the development of new accommodation does not lead to an oversupply that could impact the local housing market.

- **Community Integration**: The strategy emphasizes the importance of integrating students into the local community, promoting initiatives that encourage interaction between students and residents.

- **Sustainability**: The strategy also highlights the need for sustainable development practices in the construction and operation of PBSA, aligning with Bristol’s broader sustainability goals.

#### Conclusion

In summary, the planning policy context for purpose-built student accommodation in Bristol is shaped by a combination of national frameworks, local planning policies, and specific strategies aimed at managing the needs of the student population while ensuring community integration and sustainability. Developers seeking to establish PBSA in Bristol should carefully consider these policies and engage with local stakeholders to ensure compliance and community support.

## ES-07 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What is the planning policy context for purpose-built student accommodation in Bristol? Draw on all available planning documents.

### Output (rag)

The planning policy context for purpose-built student accommodation in Bristol is primarily outlined in the Bristol Central Area Plan (BCAP) and the Bristol Local Plan Core Strategy. Here are the key points derived from the provided extracts:

1. **Demand and Development**: The expansion of higher education in Bristol has led to a significant increase in the demand for specialist student housing. Over the past 15 years, approximately 4,500 bed spaces have been created in the city centre to help alleviate this demand (BCAP Policy BCAP4, Extract 3).

2. **Location Considerations**: While locations close to the University of Bristol are desirable for such developments, it is crucial to balance this need with the preservation of residential amenity in surrounding communities. This indicates that any proposed student accommodation must consider its impact on local residents (Extract 1 and Extract 3).

3. **Policy Framework**: The Bristol Local Plan Core Strategy and the Site Allocations and Development Management Policies provide a framework for assessing new developments. Specifically, Policy DM2 addresses residential subdivisions, shared, and specialist housing, while BCAP4 specifically focuses on specialist student housing in the city centre (Extract 1 and Extract 3).

4. **Concentration of Housing**: New developments for specialist student housing will be acceptable within the city centre unless they contribute to a harmful concentration of such housing in any given area. This policy aims to ensure a balanced mix of uses and housing types within local communities (BCAP Policy BCAP4, Extract 7).

5. **General Criteria for Development**: Although no specific sites are allocated for student accommodation outside the city centre, other locations may be suitable if they meet the general criteria outlined in the policies. This flexibility allows for potential developments in areas that can accommodate them without negatively impacting residential amenity (Extract 5 and Extract 6).

In summary, the planning policy context for purpose-built student accommodation in Bristol emphasizes the need for a balanced approach that considers both the demand for student housing and the impact on local communities. The policies encourage development in the city centre while safeguarding residential amenity and preventing an over-concentration of student housing.

## ES-08 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** Explain the 'tilted balance' in NPPF planning decisions. In what circumstances does it apply and what does it mean in practice for decision-makers?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 11, para 12 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

The 'tilted balance' is a key concept within the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) that influences decision-making in the planning process, particularly in relation to the presumption in favour of sustainable development. This concept is primarily articulated in Paragraph 11 of the NPPF, which outlines how decision-makers should approach planning applications when there is a lack of a five-year housing land supply or when policies are out-of-date.

### Circumstances in which the 'Tilted Balance' Applies

The tilted balance applies in two main scenarios:

1. **Lack of Five-Year Housing Land Supply**: When a local planning authority cannot demonstrate a five-year supply of deliverable housing sites, the presumption in favour of sustainable development is triggered. This means that planning applications for housing should be approved unless the adverse impacts of doing so would significantly and demonstrably outweigh the benefits, when assessed against the policies in the NPPF taken as a whole.

2. **Out-of-Date Policies**: The tilted balance also comes into play when relevant policies in a local plan are considered out-of-date. This can occur if the local plan is more than five years old or if it is not consistent with the NPPF. In such cases, the presumption in favour of sustainable development applies, and decision-makers are encouraged to approve applications unless there are significant adverse impacts.

### Implications for Decision-Makers

In practice, the tilted balance has several implications for decision-makers:

- **Assessment of Benefits vs. Adverse Impacts**: When the tilted balance is engaged, decision-makers must weigh the benefits of a proposed development against any adverse impacts. The benefits may include contributions to housing supply, economic growth, and social infrastructure, while adverse impacts could involve harm to the environment, local character, or amenity.

- **Greater Flexibility in Approvals**: The tilted balance effectively provides a more permissive framework for approving development proposals, particularly in areas where housing need is acute. This can lead to a higher likelihood of granting planning permission, especially for residential developments.

- **Focus on Sustainable Development**: The NPPF emphasizes that sustainable development should be at the heart of planning decisions. Therefore, even when the tilted balance is applied, developments must still align with the principles of sustainability, which include economic, social, and environmental considerations.

- **Local Plan Review and Updates**: The existence of the tilted balance underscores the importance of local authorities maintaining up-to-date local plans. Authorities are encouraged to regularly review and update their plans to ensure they can demonstrate a five-year housing land supply and that their policies remain relevant and effective.

### Conclusion

In summary, the 'tilted balance' is a crucial mechanism within the NPPF that promotes sustainable development by providing a presumption in favour of development in certain circumstances, particularly when there is a lack of housing supply or when local policies are out-of-date. For decision-makers, this means a more flexible approach to approving applications, with a focus on balancing benefits against adverse impacts while ensuring alignment with sustainable development principles. It is essential for local authorities to actively manage their housing supply and keep their planning policies current to mitigate the implications of the tilted balance.

## ES-08 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** evidence_synthesis | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** Explain the 'tilted balance' in NPPF planning decisions. In what circumstances does it apply and what does it mean in practice for decision-makers?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 11, para 12 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

The 'tilted balance' in planning decisions, as articulated in the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF), refers to the presumption in favour of sustainable development. This principle is crucial for decision-makers when assessing planning applications. According to the Liverpool Local Plan, the presumption in favour of sustainable development means that plans should be prepared on the basis that objectively assessed development needs should be met, unless the adverse impacts of doing so would significantly and demonstrably outweigh the benefits when assessed against the policies in the framework taken as a whole (Extract 1).

In practice, the 'tilted balance' applies in situations where a proposed development is not in strict accordance with the local development plan but aligns with the overarching goals of sustainable development as outlined in the NPPF. This means that if a development proposal can demonstrate that it meets the needs of the community and contributes positively to sustainable growth, it should be approved unless there are significant and demonstrable adverse impacts that outweigh these benefits (Extract 1).

The NPPF emphasizes that the planning system operates to regulate development in the community's interest while considering the development plan and other material planning considerations (Extract 3). Therefore, decision-makers must weigh the benefits of a proposed development—such as economic growth, housing supply, and community facilities—against any potential negative impacts, such as environmental degradation or loss of amenity.

In summary, the 'tilted balance' encourages a positive approach to planning, where the default position is to support sustainable development unless clear and compelling reasons exist to refuse it. This approach is intended to facilitate growth and development that meets local needs while ensuring that any adverse impacts are carefully considered and justified.

## PC-01 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** Does a proposed 80-unit residential development on a brownfield site in Bristol city centre comply with the housing policies in the NPPF December 2024? Identify which specific paragraphs apply.

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 11, para 120, para 124 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

To assess whether the proposed 80-unit residential development on a brownfield site in Bristol city centre complies with the housing policies outlined in the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) December 2024, we need to consider several key aspects of the NPPF, particularly those related to housing delivery, the use of brownfield land, and sustainable development.

### Key NPPF Policies Relevant to the Proposal

1. **Paragraph 60**: This paragraph emphasizes the government's objective to significantly boost the supply of homes. It states that to achieve this, local planning authorities should ensure that a sufficient amount and variety of land can come forward where it is needed. The proposed development of 80 units contributes to this objective, particularly in a city centre location where housing demand is typically high.

2. **Paragraph 68**: This paragraph encourages the development of small and medium-sized sites, which can be crucial for meeting housing needs. While the proposed development is larger than what is typically considered a small site, it still aligns with the NPPF's broader aim of diversifying the housing supply and providing a mix of housing types.

3. **Paragraph 74**: This paragraph states that previously developed land (brownfield sites) should be used for housing where possible. Since the proposed site is brownfield, it aligns with this policy, promoting the efficient use of land and reducing pressure on greenfield sites.

4. **Paragraph 79**: This paragraph highlights the importance of ensuring that development is sustainable and enhances the environment. The location in Bristol city centre is likely to provide access to public transport, services, and amenities, which supports sustainable living and reduces reliance on private vehicles.

5. **Paragraph 92**: This paragraph encourages the planning of healthy, inclusive, and safe places. The development should consider how it can contribute to the local community, including access to green spaces, community facilities, and ensuring safety and security for residents.

6. **Paragraph 124**: This paragraph states that planning policies and decisions should support the development of well-designed, beautiful, and sustainable buildings and places. The design of the proposed development will need to be assessed against local design policies and guidelines to ensure it contributes positively to the character of the area.

### Conclusion

Based on the above analysis, the proposed 80-unit residential development on a brownfield site in Bristol city centre appears to comply with several key housing policies outlined in the NPPF December 2024. It supports the objective of boosting housing supply (Paragraph 60), utilizes previously developed land (Paragraph 74), and is likely to promote sustainable living (Paragraph 79). 

However, it is essential to ensure that the development also meets local planning policies and guidelines, particularly regarding design, community integration, and environmental impact. A thorough assessment of the local development plan and any supplementary planning documents will be necessary to ensure full compliance and to address any potential concerns raised by stakeholders or the local community.

## PC-01 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** Does a proposed 80-unit residential development on a brownfield site in Bristol city centre comply with the housing policies in the NPPF December 2024? Identify which specific paragraphs apply.

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 11, para 120, para 124 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

To assess whether a proposed 80-unit residential development on a brownfield site in Bristol city centre complies with housing policies in the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) December 2024, we can draw on the provided context and relevant planning principles.

### Compliance with NPPF Housing Policies

1. **Use of Brownfield Land**: The NPPF encourages the use of brownfield land for new housing developments. Specifically, **NPPF paragraph 117** states that planning policies and decisions should promote an effective use of land in meeting the need for homes and other uses while safeguarding and improving the environment and ensuring safe and healthy living conditions. Since the proposed development is on a brownfield site, it aligns with this principle.

2. **Housing Mix and Type**: The Bristol Local Plan Core Strategy Policy BCS18 emphasizes the need for a variety of housing types to meet the diverse needs of the community. The proposal should demonstrate how it meets these requirements in the Design and Access Statement, as outlined in the context (Extract 1). This aligns with **NPPF paragraph 61**, which states that planning policies should reflect the needs of different groups in the community, including families with children, older people, and people with disabilities.

3. **Affordable Housing**: According to **NPPF paragraph 64**, where a need for affordable housing is identified, it should be delivered on-site unless off-site provision or a financial contribution can be robustly justified. The context mentions Development Management policy DM15, which seeks affordable housing on proposals of less than 15 homes (Extract 7). Since the proposed development consists of 80 units, it is likely that a significant proportion of affordable housing would be required, aligning with the NPPF's emphasis on delivering affordable housing.

4. **Impact on Local Character and Amenity**: The context also highlights the importance of considering the existing mix of uses in the area and the impact of changes on local character and amenity (Extract 3). This is consistent with **NPPF paragraph 130**, which states that planning policies and decisions should ensure that developments are sympathetic to local character and history, including the surrounding built environment and landscape setting.

### Conclusion

In summary, the proposed 80-unit residential development on a brownfield site in Bristol city centre appears to comply with key housing policies in the NPPF December 2024, particularly regarding the use of brownfield land, the need for a mix of housing types, the provision of affordable housing, and the consideration of local character and amenity. The specific paragraphs from the NPPF that apply include:

- **NPPF paragraph 117**: Promoting effective use of brownfield land.
- **NPPF paragraph 61**: Reflecting the needs of different community groups in housing policies.
- **NPPF paragraph 64**: Delivering affordable housing.
- **NPPF paragraph 130**: Ensuring developments are sympathetic to local character and amenity.

The proposal should be evaluated further to ensure it meets all local planning requirements and policies as outlined in the Bristol Local Plan.

## PC-02 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** A developer proposes removing a tree subject to a Tree Preservation Order to build 5 new homes. Under the NPPF and Bristol Local Plan, what is the policy position and what conditions would need to be met for consent?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 180 — source: `Bristol_Local_Plan.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

In considering the proposal to remove a tree subject to a Tree Preservation Order (TPO) in order to facilitate the construction of five new homes, it is essential to evaluate the situation against the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF), relevant Planning Practice Guidance (PPG), and the Bristol Local Plan.

### National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF)

The NPPF, particularly in Chapter 15, emphasizes the importance of conserving and enhancing the natural environment. Specifically, paragraph 175 states that:

- "When determining planning applications, local planning authorities should apply the following principles: 
  a) if significant harm to biodiversity resulting from a development cannot be avoided (through locating on an alternative site with less harmful impacts), adequately mitigated, or, as a last resort, compensated for, then planning permission should be refused."
  
- Furthermore, paragraph 179 highlights the need to protect and enhance valued landscapes, and trees are often integral to this.

### Bristol Local Plan

The Bristol Local Plan includes policies that align with the NPPF regarding the protection of trees and biodiversity. Key policies to consider include:

- **Policy DM17 (Trees and Woodlands)**: This policy states that development proposals should protect existing trees and woodlands, particularly those that are subject to TPOs. The removal of such trees will only be permitted in exceptional circumstances, where it can be demonstrated that:
  1. The tree is dead, dying, or dangerous.
  2. The removal is necessary to facilitate development that is of significant public benefit.
  3. Adequate mitigation measures are proposed, such as replacement planting or habitat creation.

### Conditions for Consent

For the developer to obtain consent for the removal of the TPO tree, they would need to meet several conditions:

1. **Justification for Removal**: The developer must provide a robust justification for the removal of the tree, demonstrating that it is essential for the proposed development and that no alternative solutions exist that would allow the development to proceed without the loss of the tree.

2. **Public Benefit**: The development must provide significant public benefits that outweigh the loss of the tree. This could include addressing housing needs, contributing to local infrastructure, or enhancing community facilities.

3. **Mitigation Measures**: The developer must propose adequate mitigation measures. This could involve:
   - Replacement planting of trees of equivalent or greater ecological value.
   - A detailed landscaping plan that enhances biodiversity and integrates new planting into the development.
   - A commitment to maintaining the new trees to ensure their establishment and growth.

4. **Ecological Assessment**: An ecological assessment may be required to evaluate the impact of the tree removal on local biodiversity and to ensure compliance with the Wildlife and Countryside Act 1981 and other relevant legislation.

5. **Consultation with Arboricultural Experts**: The developer should engage with arboricultural experts to assess the health and value of the tree and to explore potential alternatives to removal.

### Conclusion

In summary, the proposal to remove a tree subject to a TPO to facilitate the construction of five new homes must be carefully justified and supported by significant public benefits, alongside robust mitigation measures. The developer should be prepared to demonstrate compliance with both the NPPF and Bristol Local Plan policies, particularly Policy DM17, to secure planning permission. Failure to adequately address these considerations may result in refusal of the application.

## PC-02 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** A developer proposes removing a tree subject to a Tree Preservation Order to build 5 new homes. Under the NPPF and Bristol Local Plan, what is the policy position and what conditions would need to be met for consent?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 180 — source: `Bristol_Local_Plan.pdf`

### Output (rag)

In considering the proposal to remove a tree subject to a Tree Preservation Order (TPO) for the construction of five new homes, both the Bristol Local Plan and the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) provide relevant guidance.

### Policy Position

1. **Bristol Local Plan**: According to the Bristol Local Plan, specifically in the Development Management Policies, aged and veteran trees are recognized for their significant value and characteristics (Extract 1). The policy emphasizes the importance of these trees and suggests that their loss should be carefully considered. The council's Planning Obligations Supplementary Planning Document outlines the circumstances under which off-site tree provision will be necessary, indicating that if trees are to be removed, appropriate mitigation measures must be implemented (Extract 3).

2. **NPPF**: The NPPF, particularly paragraph 175c, states that development resulting in the loss or deterioration of irreplaceable habitats, such as ancient woodland and ancient or veteran trees, should be refused unless there are wholly exceptional reasons and a suitable compensation strategy exists. This aligns with the Bristol Local Plan's emphasis on the protection of significant trees (Extract 7).

### Conditions for Consent

For the developer to obtain consent for the removal of the tree subject to a TPO, the following conditions must be met:

1. **Exceptional Reasons**: The developer must demonstrate that there are wholly exceptional reasons for the removal of the tree. This could include factors such as the tree being in poor health or posing a safety risk, but these reasons must be compelling and well-documented.

2. **Compensation Strategy**: A suitable compensation strategy must be provided. This could involve the planting of replacement trees, as outlined in the Bristol Local Plan, where planning obligations will be sought to ensure that the appropriate number of replacement trees is provided (Extract 3). The developer would need to follow the approach set out in the Supplementary Planning Document regarding off-site tree provision.

3. **Tree Survey**: A British Standard 5837 Tree Survey should be conducted to assess the tree's health and value, which will inform the decision-making process regarding its removal (Extract 5).

4. **Mitigation Measures**: The developer must propose adequate mitigation measures to address the loss of the tree, which may include habitat enhancement or other ecological benefits to offset the impact of the development.

In summary, the proposal to remove a tree subject to a TPO for the construction of new homes faces significant hurdles under both the Bristol Local Plan and the NPPF. The developer must provide compelling justification for the removal and a robust compensation strategy to align with the policies aimed at protecting valuable trees.

## PC-03 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What does the NPPF December 2024 say about the sequential test for retail development outside town centres? At what floor area threshold does the impact test apply?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 90, para 91, para 92 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

As of my last update in October 2023, the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) was last revised in July 2021, and there is no NPPF dated December 2024. However, I can provide you with the relevant information based on the most current version of the NPPF and its guidance regarding retail development outside town centres.

### Sequential Test for Retail Development

The NPPF emphasizes the importance of the sequential test in assessing retail development proposals outside of town centres. According to **Paragraph 87** of the NPPF, local planning authorities are required to apply a sequential test to planning applications for main town centre uses, which include retail, leisure, and office uses, that are proposed outside of town centres. The sequential test aims to ensure that such developments are located in town centres first, then in edge-of-centre locations, and only if no suitable sites are available, in out-of-centre locations.

The key points regarding the sequential test are:

- **Preference for Town Centre Locations**: The NPPF states that local planning authorities should require applicants to demonstrate that there are no suitable sites available in town centres or edge-of-centre locations before considering out-of-centre sites.
- **Flexibility in Approach**: Applicants should be flexible in terms of the format and scale of their proposals to accommodate the sequential test.

### Impact Test Threshold

The NPPF also outlines the requirement for an impact assessment for retail developments outside of town centres. According to **Paragraph 90**, an impact assessment is required for retail developments that are not in accordance with an up-to-date development plan and that are likely to have a significant adverse impact on:

- Existing, committed, and planned public and private investment in a centre or centres in the catchment area of the proposal.
- The vitality and viability of town centres.

The impact test applies to retail developments with a gross floor area of **2,500 square metres or more**. This threshold is significant as it helps to ensure that larger retail developments, which are more likely to have a substantial impact on existing town centres, are subject to a thorough assessment.

### Conclusion

In summary, the NPPF requires a sequential test for retail developments outside town centres to prioritize town centre locations, and an impact assessment is mandated for proposals exceeding 2,500 square metres in gross floor area. These policies are designed to protect the vitality and viability of town centres and ensure sustainable development patterns.

For the most accurate and up-to-date information, it is advisable to consult the latest version of the NPPF and any relevant local planning policies, as these documents may be subject to change or updates beyond my last training cut-off.

## PC-03 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What does the NPPF December 2024 say about the sequential test for retail development outside town centres? At what floor area threshold does the impact test apply?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 90, para 91, para 92 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

The National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) December 2024 outlines the requirements for assessing retail and leisure development applications outside town centres. Specifically, it states that local planning authorities should require an impact assessment for developments that exceed a proportionate, locally set floorspace threshold. If there is no locally set threshold, the default threshold for the impact assessment is 2,500 square meters of gross floorspace (NPPF paragraph 94).

Regarding the sequential test, the NPPF emphasizes that this test should be applied to ensure that retail and leisure developments are located in town centres first, before considering edge-of-centre or out-of-centre locations. The sequential test is a critical part of the planning process to promote sustainable development and to protect the vitality and viability of town centres.

In summary:
- **Sequential Test**: Required for retail and leisure development outside town centres to prioritize town centre locations.
- **Impact Test Threshold**: Applies to developments over 2,500 square meters of gross floorspace if no local threshold is set (NPPF paragraph 94).

## PC-04 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** A planning application proposes a waste management facility in the green belt. Under NPPF policy, what exceptional circumstances would need to be demonstrated?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 152, para 153 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

In the context of the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF), the development of a waste management facility in the Green Belt is subject to strict controls, as outlined in Chapter 13, which deals with protecting Green Belt land. The NPPF establishes that the fundamental aim of Green Belt policy is to prevent urban sprawl by keeping land permanently open. 

According to Paragraph 147 of the NPPF, inappropriate development is, by definition, harmful to the Green Belt and should not be approved except in very special circumstances. For a planning application for a waste management facility to be considered acceptable in the Green Belt, the applicant must demonstrate that these very special circumstances exist. 

The following points outline the key considerations and exceptional circumstances that would need to be demonstrated:

1. **Need for the Facility**: The applicant must provide robust evidence of the need for the waste management facility. This includes demonstrating that there is a significant demand for such a facility in the area, supported by waste management strategies and local authority waste plans. The applicant should refer to the Waste Management Plan for England and any relevant local waste management strategies to substantiate this need.

2. **Lack of Alternative Sites**: The applicant must demonstrate that there are no suitable alternative sites available outside the Green Belt that could accommodate the proposed facility. This involves a thorough site selection process, including an assessment of previously developed land and other non-Green Belt locations. The applicant should provide a detailed site assessment report that considers the availability, suitability, and viability of alternative sites.

3. **Environmental and Economic Benefits**: The proposal should provide significant environmental or economic benefits that outweigh the harm to the Green Belt. This could include improvements in waste management efficiency, contributions to local employment, or advancements in sustainability practices. The applicant should present evidence of how the facility will contribute positively to local and regional waste management objectives.

4. **Mitigation of Harm**: The applicant must demonstrate how the proposal will mitigate any potential harm to the Green Belt. This could involve landscaping, design considerations, and operational practices that minimize visual and environmental impacts. The use of best practice in waste management and technology that reduces the facility's footprint may also be relevant.

5. **Compliance with Local Planning Policies**: The proposal must align with local planning policies that may provide additional context or criteria for waste management facilities. Local plans may have specific policies regarding waste management, sustainability, and Green Belt development that the applicant must address.

6. **Community Engagement**: Engaging with the local community and stakeholders can also be beneficial. Demonstrating that the proposal has taken into account local concerns and has sought to address them can strengthen the case for very special circumstances.

In summary, to justify a waste management facility in the Green Belt, the applicant must provide compelling evidence of need, lack of alternatives, significant benefits, and effective mitigation measures. Each of these elements must be thoroughly documented and supported by relevant data and analysis to meet the stringent requirements set out in the NPPF.
