## PC-04 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** A planning application proposes a waste management facility in the green belt. Under NPPF policy, what exceptional circumstances would need to be demonstrated?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 152, para 153 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

In considering a planning application for a waste management facility in the Green Belt, the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) outlines that such development is generally deemed inappropriate and should not be approved except in very special circumstances. According to NPPF paragraph 144, ‘very special circumstances’ will not exist unless the potential harm to the Green Belt by reason of inappropriateness, and any other harm resulting from the proposal, is clearly outweighed by other considerations (NPPF paragraph 144).

To demonstrate these very special circumstances for a waste management facility, the applicant would need to provide evidence that:

1. **Environmental Benefits**: The proposal could include wider environmental benefits, such as significant contributions to sustainability, reduction in landfill use, or improvements in local air quality. This aligns with the Liverpool Local Plan, which suggests that very special circumstances may include the wider environmental benefits associated with increased production of energy from renewable sources (Liverpool Local Plan, Page 233).

2. **Need for the Facility**: The applicant must demonstrate a clear need for the waste management facility that cannot be met elsewhere. This could involve showing that existing facilities are insufficient to handle the volume of waste generated in the area or that the proposed facility would serve a larger regional need.

3. **Mitigation of Harm**: The applicant should outline how any potential harm to the Green Belt will be mitigated. This could involve landscaping, design considerations, or operational practices that minimize the visual and environmental impact of the facility.

4. **Exceptional Circumstances**: The applicant must also demonstrate that there are exceptional circumstances justifying the development in the Green Belt. This could include changes in local circumstances or policies that necessitate the facility, as referenced in NPPF paragraph 83, which discusses the need for exceptional circumstances to justify changes to Green Belt boundaries.

5. **Assessment Against Green Belt Objectives**: The proposal should be assessed against the objectives of the Green Belt, ensuring that it does not conflict with the purposes of including land in the Green Belt, as highlighted in the Nottingham Local Plan (Nottingham LAPP, Page 23).

In summary, to justify a waste management facility in the Green Belt, the applicant must provide compelling evidence that the benefits of the proposal significantly outweigh the harm to the Green Belt, supported by a clear demonstration of need, environmental benefits, and mitigation strategies.

## PC-05 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What is the definition of 'grey belt' land as introduced in the NPPF December 2024, and what policy tests apply to development proposals on grey belt land?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 11c, Annex 2 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

As of my last update in October 2023, the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) does not include a specific definition of 'grey belt' land. The term 'grey belt' is not officially recognized within the NPPF or the associated Planning Practice Guidance (PPG). Instead, the NPPF primarily discusses 'green belt' land, which is designated to prevent urban sprawl and protect the countryside from encroachment.

However, if 'grey belt' land has been introduced in a subsequent version of the NPPF in December 2024, I would not have access to that information. Therefore, I recommend consulting the latest version of the NPPF directly or checking with local planning authorities for the most current definitions and policies.

In general, if a new designation like 'grey belt' has been introduced, it would likely involve specific policy tests similar to those applied to green belt land. For green belt land, the NPPF outlines several key principles and tests for development proposals, which may also apply to any new designation:

1. **Presumption Against Development**: The fundamental aim of green belt policy is to prevent urban sprawl by keeping land permanently open. Any development proposal on grey belt land would likely face a strong presumption against development unless it meets specific criteria.

2. **Exceptions to the Presumption**: The NPPF outlines certain exceptions where development may be permitted in green belt areas, such as:
   - Buildings for agriculture and forestry.
   - Facilities for outdoor sport and recreation.
   - Extensions or alterations to existing buildings, provided they do not result in disproportionate additions.
   - Limited infilling in villages.
   - Limited affordable housing for local community needs.

3. **Very Special Circumstances**: For any development that does not fall within the exceptions, the applicant would need to demonstrate 'very special circumstances' that outweigh the harm to the green belt (or grey belt) and any other harm resulting from the proposal.

4. **Impact on Openness**: Any development proposal would need to consider its impact on the openness of the grey belt land, similar to the considerations for green belt land.

5. **Local Plan Policies**: Local planning authorities may have specific policies regarding grey belt land in their local plans, which would need to be adhered to in addition to national policy.

In summary, while I cannot provide specific details about 'grey belt' land as defined in the NPPF December 2024, it is essential to refer to the latest NPPF and local planning policies for accurate guidance. If 'grey belt' has been introduced, it would likely involve a presumption against development, with specific exceptions and requirements for demonstrating very special circumstances, similar to the existing framework for green belt land.

## PC-05 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What is the definition of 'grey belt' land as introduced in the NPPF December 2024, and what policy tests apply to development proposals on grey belt land?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 11c, Annex 2 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

The definition of 'grey belt' land, as introduced in the NPPF December 2024, is specified as land within the Green Belt that comprises previously developed land and/or any other land that does not strongly contribute to the purposes outlined in paragraph 143 of the NPPF (which includes purposes such as preventing urban sprawl and safeguarding the countryside from encroachment). It is important to note that 'grey belt' excludes land where the application of policies relating to areas or assets in footnote 7 (other than Green Belt) would provide a strong reason for refusing development (NPPF December 2024, Page 73).

In terms of policy tests that apply to development proposals on grey belt land, the NPPF indicates that while grey belt land may be less strictly protected than other Green Belt areas, any development must still be assessed against the overarching principles of Green Belt policy. This means that development proposals must demonstrate that they do not conflict with the purposes of the Green Belt, and they must also comply with any relevant local planning policies that may apply to the specific site in question.

Furthermore, local planning authorities are required to establish Green Belt boundaries in their Local Plans, which set the framework for Green Belt and settlement policy. These boundaries should only be altered in exceptional circumstances, as outlined in the NPPF (Nottingham LAPP 2020, Page 23, Paragraph 2.35; Newcastle-Gateshead CSUCP 2010-2030, Page 109, Paragraph 12.56). Therefore, any development on grey belt land must also consider whether it aligns with the established Green Belt boundaries and the exceptional circumstances required for any alterations to those boundaries. 

In summary, while grey belt land may offer some flexibility for development, it remains subject to the fundamental principles of Green Belt protection and local planning policies.

## PC-06 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** A 200-bed hotel is proposed adjacent to Bristol Airport in the green belt. Assess its compliance with NPPF policy on development in the green belt and any relevant exceptions.

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 147, para 149 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

To assess the proposed 200-bed hotel adjacent to Bristol Airport in the green belt, we must refer to the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF), particularly Chapter 13, which deals with protecting the Green Belt.

### Green Belt Policy Overview

The NPPF establishes that the fundamental aim of Green Belt policy is to prevent urban sprawl by keeping land permanently open. The five purposes of the Green Belt, as outlined in paragraph 138 of the NPPF, are:

1. To check the unrestricted sprawl of large built-up areas.
2. To prevent neighbouring towns from merging into one another.
3. To assist in safeguarding the countryside from encroachment.
4. To preserve the setting and special character of historic towns.
5. To assist in urban regeneration, by encouraging the recycling of derelict and other urban land.

### Development in the Green Belt

Paragraph 147 of the NPPF states that inappropriate development is, by definition, harmful to the Green Belt and should not be approved except in very special circumstances. The proposed hotel must therefore be assessed against the criteria for what constitutes appropriate development in the Green Belt.

### Exceptions to Inappropriate Development

Paragraph 149 of the NPPF outlines the types of development that are not considered inappropriate in the Green Belt, which include:

- **Buildings for agriculture and forestry.**
- **Provision of essential facilities for outdoor sport and recreation.**
- **The extension or alteration of a building, provided it does not result in disproportionate additions.**
- **The replacement of a building, provided the new building is in the same use and not materially larger.**
- **Limited infilling in villages.**
- **Limited affordable housing for local community needs.**
- **Development that would not have a greater impact on the openness of the Green Belt than the existing development.**

### Assessment of the Proposed Hotel

1. **Inappropriate Development**: A 200-bed hotel does not fall under any of the categories listed in paragraph 149 as appropriate development. Therefore, it is classified as inappropriate development in the Green Belt.

2. **Very Special Circumstances**: To justify the approval of this inappropriate development, the applicant must demonstrate very special circumstances that outweigh the harm to the Green Belt. This could include:
   - Economic benefits, such as job creation and support for local businesses.
   - The need for hotel accommodation linked to Bristol Airport, which may be argued as essential for the airport's operation and growth.
   - Any potential benefits to the local community or infrastructure improvements that could arise from the development.

3. **Impact on Openness**: The proposal must also be assessed for its impact on the openness of the Green Belt. A 200-bed hotel is likely to have a significant physical presence and could detract from the openness of the area, which is a key consideration in determining the application.

4. **Local Planning Policy**: It is also essential to consider local planning policies that may provide additional context or restrictions regarding development in the Green Belt. Local plans may have specific policies that align with or expand upon the NPPF, and these should be reviewed to ensure compliance.

### Conclusion

In summary, the proposed 200-bed hotel adjacent to Bristol Airport is classified as inappropriate development in the Green Belt according to the NPPF. The applicant must provide compelling very special circumstances to justify the development, demonstrating that the benefits outweigh the harm to the Green Belt. Additionally, the impact on openness must be carefully considered, alongside any relevant local planning policies. Without a robust justification, the proposal is unlikely to gain approval under current NPPF guidelines.

## PC-06 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** A 200-bed hotel is proposed adjacent to Bristol Airport in the green belt. Assess its compliance with NPPF policy on development in the green belt and any relevant exceptions.

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 147, para 149 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

To assess the compliance of a proposed 200-bed hotel adjacent to Bristol Airport in the Green Belt with the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) and relevant local planning policies, we must consider the following key points:

1. **Green Belt Policy**: The NPPF outlines that the fundamental aim of Green Belt policy is to prevent urban sprawl by keeping land permanently open. It states that inappropriate development is, by definition, harmful to the Green Belt and should not be approved except in very special circumstances (NPPF paragraph 143). 

2. **Exceptions to Inappropriate Development**: The NPPF provides specific exceptions where development may be considered appropriate in the Green Belt, including:
   - Buildings for agriculture and forestry.
   - Facilities for outdoor sport and recreation.
   - The extension or alteration of existing buildings.
   - The replacement of a building, provided the new building is not materially larger than the one it replaces.
   - Limited infilling in villages.
   - Limited affordable housing for local community needs (NPPF paragraph 145).

3. **Local Policy Context**: According to the Core Strategy, sites in the Green Belt will not be released for development unless a need for additional housing land is established through monitoring of housing need and demand (Core Strategy, Page 63). This indicates a strong presumption against development in the Green Belt unless specific criteria are met.

4. **Assessment of the Hotel Proposal**: The proposed hotel does not fall under any of the exceptions listed in the NPPF for appropriate development in the Green Belt. It is not an agricultural building, nor does it serve outdoor recreation or community needs in a manner that would justify its location in the Green Belt. Furthermore, the Core Strategy emphasizes that Green Belt sites will not be released for development unless there is a demonstrated need for housing, which does not apply to a hotel.

5. **Conclusion**: Given the information provided, the proposed 200-bed hotel adjacent to Bristol Airport in the Green Belt would be classified as inappropriate development under both NPPF policy and local planning policy. There is no indication of "very special circumstances" that would justify this development, and it does not meet the criteria for exceptions outlined in the NPPF. Therefore, the proposal is unlikely to comply with the relevant planning policies and would face significant challenges in obtaining planning permission. 

In summary, the proposal for a hotel in the Green Belt adjacent to Bristol Airport does not align with NPPF policies regarding inappropriate development and lacks the necessary justification to be considered acceptable under local planning policies.

## PC-07 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What affordable housing obligations does the NPPF require for residential developments? What is the policy position on viability assessments for affordable housing?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 65, para 66, para 67 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

The National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF), which was first published in 2012 and updated in 2021, sets out the government's planning policies for England and how these are expected to be applied. One of the key areas addressed in the NPPF is affordable housing, which is a critical component of sustainable development.

### Affordable Housing Obligations

**1. Definition and Requirement:**
The NPPF defines affordable housing as housing for sale or rent, for those whose needs are not met by the market. This includes social rented, affordable rented, and intermediate housing. The NPPF emphasizes the need for local planning authorities (LPAs) to plan for a mix of housing, including affordable housing, to meet the needs of their communities.

**2. Local Plans and Targets:**
According to Paragraph 61 of the NPPF, LPAs are required to assess the housing needs of their area, including the need for affordable housing. They should set out clear policies on affordable housing in their Local Plans, specifying the proportion of affordable housing required on residential developments. This is often expressed as a percentage of the total number of homes to be delivered on-site.

**3. Thresholds:**
The NPPF (Paragraph 64) states that where major developments (typically defined as 10 or more dwellings) are proposed, LPAs should expect at least 10% of the total number of homes to be affordable, unless it can be demonstrated that this would make the development unviable. However, local policies may set different thresholds based on local circumstances.

### Viability Assessments

**1. General Policy Position:**
The NPPF acknowledges that the viability of a development is a crucial consideration when determining planning applications. Paragraph 57 states that LPAs should consider the viability of a development when assessing planning applications, particularly in relation to affordable housing contributions.

**2. Viability Assessments:**
The NPPF allows for viability assessments to be submitted by developers to demonstrate that the provision of affordable housing (or other planning obligations) would render a development unviable. However, the NPPF also emphasizes that these assessments should be transparent and publicly available, and that LPAs should have clear policies on how they will assess viability.

**3. Local Plan Policies:**
LPAs are encouraged to set out their approach to viability in their Local Plans, including the circumstances under which viability assessments will be required and how they will be assessed. This is further elaborated in the Planning Practice Guidance (PPG), which provides detailed guidance on how to assess viability, including the use of the RICS (Royal Institution of Chartered Surveyors) guidance on financial viability.

**4. Importance of Affordable Housing:**
The NPPF (Paragraph 62) stresses that the need for affordable housing should be a key consideration in the planning process, and that LPAs should be proactive in seeking to secure affordable housing contributions. Viability assessments should not be used as a means to avoid providing affordable housing where it is feasible to do so.

### Conclusion

In summary, the NPPF requires LPAs to plan for affordable housing through their Local Plans, setting clear targets and thresholds for affordable housing contributions in residential developments. Viability assessments are permitted to demonstrate when affordable housing obligations may not be feasible, but these assessments must be transparent and aligned with local policies. The overarching aim is to ensure that affordable housing needs are met while balancing the viability of development projects.

## PC-07 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What affordable housing obligations does the NPPF require for residential developments? What is the policy position on viability assessments for affordable housing?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 65, para 66, para 67 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

The National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) outlines specific obligations regarding affordable housing for residential developments. According to the NPPF, where major development involving housing is proposed, planning policies and decisions should ensure that the mix of affordable housing meets identified local needs. This includes a range of tenures such as Social Rent, other affordable housing for rent, and affordable home ownership (NPPF paragraph 66).

Furthermore, the NPPF acknowledges that when it comes to brownfield land, where vacant buildings are being reused or redeveloped, any affordable housing contribution required should be proportionately reduced (NPPF paragraph 18). This indicates a flexible approach to affordable housing obligations, allowing for adjustments based on the specific context of the development site.

Regarding viability assessments, the context provided indicates that planning obligations, including those related to affordable housing, must consider the viability of the scheme. For instance, the Manchester Core Strategy mentions that the need for additional housing provision, including affordable housing, is dependent on the results of a financial viability assessment of the scheme (Manchester Core Strategy, Page 119). This suggests that if a development's viability is compromised by the obligations imposed, adjustments may be necessary to ensure that the development can proceed.

In summary, the NPPF requires that affordable housing obligations are tailored to meet local needs and allows for flexibility in contributions based on site conditions, while viability assessments play a crucial role in determining the feasibility of these obligations.

## PC-08 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** easy

**Task:** A householder wants to add a rear extension. Under permitted development rights and the NPPF, what are the key size and impact criteria that determine whether planning permission is required?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 230 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

When considering a householder's proposal to add a rear extension under permitted development rights in the UK, it is essential to refer to the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF), the Planning Practice Guidance (PPG), and the specific permitted development rights outlined in the Town and Country Planning (General Permitted Development) (England) Order 2015 (as amended).

### Key Size and Impact Criteria

1. **Permitted Development Rights**:
   Under the General Permitted Development Order (GPDO), certain extensions can be constructed without the need for full planning permission, provided they meet specific criteria. The relevant criteria for a rear extension are as follows:

   - **Single-storey Extensions**:
     - The extension must not exceed 4 meters in height.
     - The extension must not extend beyond the rear wall of the original house by more than 3 meters for detached houses, or 3 meters for semi-detached and terraced houses.
     - The maximum height of the eaves must not exceed 3 meters.
     - The extension must not cover more than half the area of land around the original house.

   - **Double-storey Extensions**:
     - The extension must not exceed 3 meters in height.
     - It must not extend beyond the rear wall of the original house by more than 3 meters.
     - The extension must not be within 7 meters of the rear boundary of the property.

2. **Impact on Neighbours**:
   Even if an extension falls within the permitted development criteria, it must not adversely affect the amenity of neighbouring properties. This includes considerations of:
   - **Light**: The extension should not significantly overshadow adjacent properties.
   - **Privacy**: The design should avoid overlooking or loss of privacy to neighbouring gardens and windows.
   - **Visual Impact**: The extension should be in keeping with the character of the existing dwelling and the surrounding area.

3. **Design and Appearance**:
   The extension should be designed to be in harmony with the existing building. This includes:
   - Use of similar materials and architectural styles.
   - Consideration of the overall scale and massing in relation to the original house.

4. **Article 4 Directions**:
   In some areas, local authorities may have removed permitted development rights through Article 4 Directions. It is crucial to check whether such restrictions apply to the property in question, as this would require a full planning application regardless of the size of the extension.

5. **Local Planning Policies**:
   Local planning authorities may have specific policies that further guide the development of extensions. It is advisable to consult the local development plan or supplementary planning documents for any additional criteria that may apply.

### Conclusion

In summary, a householder wishing to add a rear extension under permitted development rights must ensure that the proposal adheres to the size limitations and impact criteria outlined above. If the extension exceeds these limits or if there are local restrictions in place, planning permission will be required. It is always prudent to engage with the local planning authority early in the process to clarify any specific local policies or requirements that may affect the proposal.

## PC-08 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** policy_compliance | **Difficulty:** easy

**Task:** A householder wants to add a rear extension. Under permitted development rights and the NPPF, what are the key size and impact criteria that determine whether planning permission is required?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 230 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

To determine whether planning permission is required for a householder wishing to add a rear extension under permitted development rights, we can refer to the relevant planning documents and the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF).

1. **Size Criteria**: 
   - According to the context provided, the NPPF and local planning policies typically outline specific size thresholds that, if exceeded, would necessitate planning permission. While the extracts do not provide explicit dimensions for householder extensions, they do reference general thresholds for development:
     - Development on a site having an area of 0.5 hectares or more, or
     - The provision of a building or buildings where the floor space to be created is 1,000 square metres or more (Extract 1 and 2).
   - For household extensions specifically, the general permitted development rights allow for extensions up to 3 metres in depth for single-storey rear extensions on terraced houses and up to 4 metres for detached houses, without the need for planning permission, provided they do not exceed these limits.

2. **Impact Criteria**:
   - The NPPF emphasizes the importance of protecting local amenity and the well-being of the area (NPPF paragraph 55). This means that any extension must not significantly harm the amenity of neighboring properties, which includes considerations such as overshadowing, loss of privacy, and visual impact.
   - Additionally, local planning policies may require that significant harmful ecological impacts are avoided, and if they cannot be avoided, they should be mitigated (Nottingham LAPP, Extract 5 and 6). While this is more relevant to larger developments, it underscores the need for careful consideration of the environmental impact of any extension.

3. **Permitted Development Rights**:
   - Extensions that fall within the remit of the Town and Country Planning (General Permitted Development) (England) Order 2015 (as amended) are typically exempt from needing full planning permission, provided they meet the specified criteria (Liverpool Local Plan, Extract 3 and 4). 

In summary, for a householder considering a rear extension, the key size criteria are that the extension must not exceed the specified depth limits for permitted development. The impact criteria focus on ensuring that the extension does not harm the amenity of neighboring properties or the local environment. If these criteria are met, planning permission may not be required. However, if the proposed extension exceeds these limits or has a significant negative impact, planning permission would be necessary.

## SA-01 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** A 2-hectare brownfield site in south Bristol is proposed for 120 homes. It is within flood zone 2 and adjacent to a Grade II listed building. What are the key planning considerations and which policies apply?

### Output (baseline)

When assessing the proposed development of 120 homes on a 2-hectare brownfield site in south Bristol, several key planning considerations must be taken into account, particularly given the site's location within flood zone 2 and its proximity to a Grade II listed building. Below are the primary considerations and relevant policies:

### 1. **Flood Risk Assessment**
Given that the site is located in flood zone 2, which is defined as an area at risk of flooding from rivers or the sea, a comprehensive Flood Risk Assessment (FRA) is essential. According to the **National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF)**, particularly **Paragraph 159**, development should be directed away from areas at highest risk of flooding. The FRA should demonstrate that:

- The development will be safe for its lifetime, without increasing flood risk elsewhere.
- Appropriate mitigation measures are in place, such as sustainable drainage systems (SuDS) to manage surface water runoff.
- The Sequential Test and, if necessary, the Exception Test are satisfied, as outlined in **NPPF Paragraphs 162-165**.

### 2. **Heritage Considerations**
The proximity to a Grade II listed building introduces significant heritage considerations. The **NPPF** emphasizes the importance of conserving and enhancing the historic environment (see **Paragraphs 189-202**). Key points include:

- Assessing the impact of the proposed development on the significance of the listed building and its setting.
- Ensuring that any development does not harm the character or appearance of the area, particularly in relation to the listed building.
- Engaging with local heritage policies, which may be outlined in Bristol's Local Plan or supplementary planning documents.

### 3. **Local Planning Policies**
Local planning policies will also play a crucial role in the assessment of this application. Bristol City Council's Local Plan and any relevant supplementary planning documents should be reviewed for specific policies regarding:

- Housing delivery and density, ensuring that the proposed number of homes aligns with local housing needs and targets.
- Design standards, particularly in relation to the character of the surrounding area and the listed building.
- Environmental considerations, including biodiversity and green space requirements.

### 4. **Sustainability and Infrastructure**
The development must also consider sustainability principles, as outlined in **NPPF Paragraph 8**, which promotes sustainable development that meets economic, social, and environmental objectives. Key considerations include:

- Accessibility to public transport, services, and amenities to reduce reliance on private vehicles.
- Incorporating energy-efficient designs and renewable energy sources to meet local and national sustainability targets.
- Engaging with local infrastructure providers to ensure that necessary services (e.g., schools, healthcare) can accommodate the additional population.

### 5. **Community Engagement**
Engagement with the local community is essential, as outlined in **NPPF Paragraph 128**. The developer should consider conducting pre-application consultations to gather feedback and address any concerns from local residents and stakeholders.

### Conclusion
In summary, the proposed development of 120 homes on the brownfield site in south Bristol requires careful consideration of flood risk, heritage impacts, local planning policies, sustainability, and community engagement. A thorough assessment against the NPPF and local policies will be necessary to ensure that the proposal is compliant and can be supported by the local planning authority. It is advisable to work closely with planning officers and heritage consultants to navigate these considerations effectively.

## SA-01 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** A 2-hectare brownfield site in south Bristol is proposed for 120 homes. It is within flood zone 2 and adjacent to a Grade II listed building. What are the key planning considerations and which policies apply?

### Output (rag)

In assessing the proposed development of a 2-hectare brownfield site in south Bristol for 120 homes, several key planning considerations arise, particularly in relation to flood risk and heritage conservation, as well as compliance with local planning policies.

### Key Planning Considerations:

1. **Flood Risk Assessment**:
   - The site is located within flood zone 2, which indicates a medium risk of flooding. According to the Bristol Local Plan Core Strategy, specifically Policy BCS16 (Flood Risk and Water Management), any development on sites that are at risk of flooding or larger than one hectare must be supported by a Flood Risk Assessment (FRA). This requirement is reiterated in the extracts from the Site Allocations and Development Management Policies, which state that a site-specific flood risk assessment is necessary for sites greater than 1 hectare (Extracts 3, 5). Therefore, the developer must conduct an FRA to demonstrate how the risks will be managed and mitigated.

2. **Heritage Considerations**:
   - The proximity of the site to a Grade II listed building introduces additional considerations under the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) and local policies regarding heritage assets. The NPPF emphasizes the need to conserve and enhance the historic environment (NPPF paragraph 189). The development must ensure that it does not adversely affect the setting of the listed building, which may involve assessments of visual impact and architectural compatibility.

3. **Housing Allocation**:
   - The proposed development of 120 homes aligns with the local planning objectives to provide housing, as indicated in the context provided. The estimated number of homes for similar sites (e.g., NP12 in Brislington with an estimated 100 homes) suggests that the local planning authority is supportive of residential developments in appropriate contexts (Extracts 3, 5).

4. **Sustainability and Urban Design**:
   - The development should also adhere to principles of quality urban design as outlined in Policy BCS21 (Quality Urban Design) of the Bristol Local Plan Core Strategy. This includes considerations of layout, density, and integration with the surrounding area to create a sustainable and attractive living environment.

### Applicable Policies:
- **BCS16**: Flood Risk and Water Management – Requires a Flood Risk Assessment for developments in flood risk areas.
- **BCS21**: Quality Urban Design – Emphasizes the importance of good design in new developments.
- **NPPF**: National Planning Policy Framework – Particularly relevant sections on flood risk management and the conservation of heritage assets.

### Conclusion:
In summary, the proposed development of 120 homes on the 2-hectare brownfield site must address flood risk through a comprehensive Flood Risk Assessment, consider the impact on the adjacent Grade II listed building, and comply with local policies regarding housing and urban design. Engaging with these considerations early in the planning process will be crucial for the success of the application.

## SA-02 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** Assess the suitability of a site in the Bristol green belt for a rural exception housing scheme. What policy tests must be passed and what types of housing would be acceptable?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 78, para 79 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

To assess the suitability of a site in the Bristol Green Belt for a rural exception housing scheme, it is essential to consider both national and local planning policies, particularly those related to Green Belt development and rural exception housing.

### National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF)

1. **Green Belt Policy**: The NPPF (2019) outlines the purposes of the Green Belt in Paragraph 138, which include:
   - To check the unrestricted sprawl of large built-up areas.
   - To prevent neighboring towns from merging into one another.
   - To assist in safeguarding the countryside from encroachment.
   - To preserve the setting and special character of historic towns.
   - To assist in urban regeneration by encouraging the recycling of derelict and other urban land.

   Development in the Green Belt is generally considered inappropriate unless it meets specific exceptions outlined in Paragraph 149. These exceptions include:
   - Limited infilling in villages.
   - Limited affordable housing for local community needs (under certain circumstances).

2. **Rural Exception Housing**: The NPPF supports rural exception sites in Paragraph 77, which states that local planning authorities should be responsive to local circumstances and plan housing development to reflect local needs, particularly for affordable housing. Rural exception sites are intended to provide affordable housing to meet local needs and should be located within or adjacent to existing settlements.

### Local Planning Policy

In Bristol, the local planning policy framework will also play a crucial role in determining the suitability of the site. The Bristol Local Plan and any relevant supplementary planning documents (SPDs) should be reviewed for specific policies regarding Green Belt development and rural exception housing.

1. **Bristol Local Plan**: The local plan will likely contain policies that align with the NPPF but may also provide additional criteria for assessing rural exception housing. It is essential to check for:
   - Definitions of what constitutes a rural exception site.
   - Criteria for assessing local need for affordable housing.
   - Any specific designations or constraints on the site.

### Policy Tests for Rural Exception Housing

To successfully develop a rural exception housing scheme in the Bristol Green Belt, the following policy tests must be passed:

1. **Demonstration of Local Need**: The applicant must provide robust evidence of local housing need, typically through a housing needs assessment. This should demonstrate that there is a demand for affordable housing that cannot be met through existing supply.

2. **Site Suitability**: The site must be suitable for development in terms of access to services and facilities, environmental impact, and compatibility with surrounding land uses. It should ideally be located adjacent to an existing settlement to minimize the impact on the Green Belt.

3. **Affordable Housing Provision**: The scheme must primarily provide affordable housing. The NPPF defines affordable housing as housing for sale or rent, for those whose needs are not met by the market. This includes:
   - Affordable rented housing.
   - Starter homes.
   - Discounted market sales housing.
   - Other affordable routes to home ownership.

4. **Minimal Impact on Green Belt**: The development must demonstrate that it will not have a significant adverse impact on the purposes of the Green Belt. This may involve careful design and layout to ensure that the development is not visually intrusive and maintains the openness of the Green Belt.

5. **Community Support**: Evidence of community support for the proposal can strengthen the case for a rural exception site. This may involve consultations with local residents and stakeholders.

### Acceptable Types of Housing

The types of housing that would be acceptable in a rural exception housing scheme typically include:

- **Affordable Rented Housing**: Units available for rent at a cost that is affordable to those on low incomes.
- **Shared Ownership**: Housing that allows individuals to purchase a share of the property while renting the remaining share.
- **Discounted Market Sales Housing**: Homes sold at a discount to the market rate, aimed at first-time buyers or those unable to afford full market prices.
- **Self-Build Opportunities**: If aligned with local needs, self-build projects may also be considered.

### Conclusion

In summary, while a rural exception housing scheme in the Bristol Green Belt is possible, it must meet specific policy tests related to local need, site suitability, and minimal impact on the Green Belt. The focus should be on providing affordable housing options that cater to the needs of the local community. A thorough assessment of both national and local policies, alongside community engagement, will be crucial in advancing such a proposal.

## SA-02 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** Assess the suitability of a site in the Bristol green belt for a rural exception housing scheme. What policy tests must be passed and what types of housing would be acceptable?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 78, para 79 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

To assess the suitability of a site in the Bristol Green Belt for a rural exception housing scheme, several policy tests must be considered based on the provided planning documents.

1. **Need for Additional Housing Land**: According to the Core Strategy, sites in the Green Belt will not be released for development unless a need for additional housing land is established through monitoring of housing need and demand, as well as economic growth (Core Strategy, Page 63). Therefore, the first test is to demonstrate that there is a specific need for housing that cannot be met within existing urban areas.

2. **Rural Exception Policy**: While the provided documents do not explicitly mention a rural exception policy, such policies typically allow for small-scale affordable housing developments in rural areas, provided they meet local needs. It is essential to ensure that any proposed scheme aligns with the principles of rural exception housing, which usually focuses on providing affordable housing for local people.

3. **Mix of Uses**: The Bristol City Centre policies emphasize the importance of a sufficient mix of uses in any development proposal (BCAP, Page 16). While this is more relevant to urban settings, it suggests that any rural exception housing scheme should consider the existing mix of uses in the area and how the proposed development would impact the character and amenity of the locality.

4. **Impact on Residential Amenity**: The documents highlight the need to balance new housing developments against the preservation of residential amenity (BCAP, Page 19). This is particularly relevant if the site is near existing residential areas, as the development should not adversely affect the quality of life for current residents.

5. **Types of Housing Acceptable**: The context does not specify the types of housing that would be acceptable for a rural exception scheme. However, based on general UK planning policy, such schemes typically focus on affordable housing, including social rent and shared ownership, aimed at meeting the needs of local residents.

In summary, to proceed with a rural exception housing scheme in the Bristol Green Belt, the following policy tests must be passed:
- Establish a demonstrable need for additional housing land.
- Ensure the proposal aligns with the principles of rural exception housing.
- Consider the existing mix of uses and the impact on local character and amenity.
- Focus on providing affordable housing options for local residents.

These considerations will guide the assessment of the site's suitability for the proposed development.

## SA-03 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** A site in Bristol city centre is currently vacant office space and is proposed for conversion to 30 residential units. What policy considerations apply to the change of use?

### Output (baseline)

When considering the proposed conversion of a vacant office space in Bristol city centre to 30 residential units, several key policy considerations must be taken into account, including national, regional, and local planning policies. Below is a detailed analysis of the relevant policies and considerations:

### 1. **National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF)**

The NPPF provides a framework for planning decisions in England and emphasizes the importance of sustainable development. Key sections relevant to this proposal include:

- **Section 5: Delivering a sufficient supply of homes**: This section highlights the need to significantly boost the supply of homes. The conversion of vacant office space to residential units can contribute to meeting local housing needs, particularly in urban areas where there is a demand for housing.

- **Section 11: Making effective use of land**: The NPPF encourages the use of previously developed land and the efficient use of land. Converting an existing office building aligns with this principle, especially in a city centre location where land is at a premium.

- **Section 12: Achieving well-designed places**: The proposal must ensure that the residential units are of high quality and contribute positively to the local character and appearance of the area. This includes considerations of design, layout, and amenity for future residents.

### 2. **Planning Practice Guidance (PPG)**

The PPG provides further detail on the implementation of the NPPF. Relevant guidance includes:

- **Change of Use**: The PPG outlines that changes of use from commercial to residential may be supported, particularly where the commercial use is no longer viable. Evidence may be required to demonstrate that the office space has been marketed for its intended use without success.

- **Housing Needs Assessment**: Local authorities are encouraged to assess housing needs and ensure that developments contribute to meeting these needs. The proposal should align with Bristol's housing strategy and local housing needs assessments.

### 3. **Local Planning Policy**

Bristol City Council's Local Plan and any relevant supplementary planning documents (SPDs) will provide specific policies that must be adhered to. Key considerations include:

- **Bristol Local Plan**: The Local Plan will contain policies regarding residential development, including density, design, and amenity standards. It is essential to review policies related to residential conversions, particularly in the context of the city centre.

- **Affordable Housing**: Depending on the size and nature of the development, there may be a requirement for a percentage of the units to be affordable housing. This is in line with local policy objectives to provide a mix of housing types.

- **Sustainability and Environmental Considerations**: The proposal must comply with local sustainability policies, which may include energy efficiency standards, waste management, and the provision of green space or communal areas.

### 4. **Permitted Development Rights**

It is also important to consider whether the conversion can be undertaken under permitted development rights. The Town and Country Planning (General Permitted Development) (England) Order 2015 allows for certain changes of use without the need for full planning permission, particularly from office to residential (Class O). However, this is subject to specific conditions and limitations, including:

- **Prior Approval**: If the conversion falls under permitted development, the applicant may still need to apply for prior approval to assess the impact on transport, contamination, flooding, and the provision of adequate natural light.

### 5. **Community Engagement and Consultation**

Engaging with the local community and stakeholders is also a critical aspect of the planning process. This can help identify any concerns regarding the proposed development and ensure that the project aligns with community aspirations.

### Conclusion

In summary, the proposed conversion of vacant office space to residential units in Bristol city centre must consider national policies outlined in the NPPF, guidance from the PPG, and specific local planning policies. It is essential to demonstrate how the proposal meets housing needs, contributes to sustainable development, and adheres to design and amenity standards. Additionally, the potential for permitted development rights should be explored, along with community engagement to ensure a successful planning outcome.

## SA-03 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** A site in Bristol city centre is currently vacant office space and is proposed for conversion to 30 residential units. What policy considerations apply to the change of use?

### Output (rag)

When considering the proposed conversion of a vacant office space in Bristol city centre to 30 residential units, several key policy considerations from the provided planning documents must be taken into account:

1. **Operational Constraints on Ground Floor Use**: According to the Bristol Central Area Plan (BCAP), the conversion of upper floors to residential use is unlikely to be acceptable if it imposes undue operational constraints on the ground floor use that could jeopardize its viability. This means that the proposed residential units must not interfere with any existing or potential ground floor commercial activities (BCAP, Extract 1 & 2).

2. **Access Requirements**: The BCAP also stipulates that suitable, safe, and independent access to the residential units must be provided. This is crucial to ensure that the residential use does not compromise the safety and accessibility of the building for both residents and any ground floor commercial operations (BCAP, Extract 1 & 2).

3. **Mixed-Use Development Considerations**: The site is described as partly vacant and underused, and it has been promoted for mixed-use development. This aligns with the regeneration aspirations outlined in Core Strategy Policy BCS1, which encourages a mix of housing types, sizes, and tenures while preserving employment opportunities. The mixed-use approach is seen as beneficial for increasing the likelihood of successful delivery of the proposed allocation (Site Allocations and Development Management Policies, Extract 5 & 6).

4. **Context of Existing Office Space**: The BCAP highlights that Bristol city centre has core office areas that provide a strong critical mass of high-quality office floorspace. The conversion of office space to residential use should be carefully considered in the context of maintaining this critical mass and ensuring that the city centre continues to meet modern business needs (BCAP, Extract 7 & 8).

5. **Potential for Intensification of Use**: The documents suggest that there may be opportunities for further intensification of use in the city centre. However, any proposed conversion must still adhere to the overarching policies that prioritize the viability of existing commercial uses and the overall character of the area (BCAP, Extract 4 & 8).

In summary, the proposed conversion of the vacant office space to residential units must ensure that it does not adversely affect ground floor commercial viability, provides safe and independent access, aligns with mixed-use development aspirations, and considers the broader context of office space in the city centre. These considerations are essential to comply with the relevant planning policies and to support sustainable urban development in Bristol.

## SA-04 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What key transport and accessibility criteria must a large residential development site in Bristol satisfy under the local plan and NPPF?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 114, para 115 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

When assessing a large residential development site in Bristol, it is essential to consider both the local planning policies and the overarching principles set out in the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF). Below are the key transport and accessibility criteria that must be satisfied:

### 1. **Sustainable Transport Modes (NPPF Paragraph 104)**
The NPPF emphasizes the need to promote sustainable transport. Large residential developments should be located where they can be accessed by a range of transport modes, including walking, cycling, public transport, and private vehicles. The development should:
- Prioritize walking and cycling by providing safe and direct routes.
- Ensure proximity to public transport services, such as bus and train stations, to reduce reliance on car travel.

### 2. **Accessibility to Services and Facilities (NPPF Paragraph 105)**
The site should be within reasonable walking distance of essential services and facilities, such as schools, healthcare, shops, and recreational areas. This is crucial for promoting a sustainable community where residents can meet their daily needs without excessive travel.

### 3. **Transport Assessments and Statements (NPPF Paragraph 111)**
A Transport Assessment (TA) is typically required for large developments to evaluate the impact of the proposed development on the transport network. The TA should:
- Assess existing transport conditions and forecast future conditions with the development in place.
- Identify any necessary mitigation measures to address potential transport impacts.

### 4. **Local Plan Policies**
Bristol's Local Plan, particularly the Bristol Core Strategy and the Site Allocations and Development Management Policies, outlines specific transport and accessibility requirements. Key considerations include:
- **Policy BCS10 (Transport and Access)**: This policy promotes sustainable transport and requires developments to contribute to the transport network's improvement. Developers may need to provide transport infrastructure improvements or financial contributions towards public transport enhancements.
- **Policy DM24 (Transport and Accessibility)**: This policy requires developments to demonstrate that they will not have an unacceptable impact on the transport network and that they will promote sustainable transport options.

### 5. **Parking Standards (Local Plan)**
The local plan will specify parking standards for residential developments. It is essential to ensure that the proposed parking provision aligns with these standards, promoting a balance between sufficient parking and encouraging sustainable transport use.

### 6. **Travel Plans (NPPF Paragraph 111)**
Developers may be required to submit a Travel Plan that outlines measures to promote sustainable travel among residents. This plan should include strategies for reducing car dependency and encouraging walking, cycling, and the use of public transport.

### 7. **Impact on Local Transport Infrastructure**
The development should not adversely affect the existing transport infrastructure. This includes ensuring that local roads can accommodate additional traffic and that any necessary upgrades to public transport facilities are identified and planned.

### 8. **Community Engagement**
Engagement with local communities and stakeholders is vital to understand transport needs and concerns. This aligns with the NPPF's emphasis on plan-making that reflects local priorities (NPPF Paragraph 39).

### Conclusion
In summary, a large residential development site in Bristol must satisfy a range of transport and accessibility criteria as outlined in the NPPF and local planning policies. These include promoting sustainable transport modes, ensuring accessibility to services, conducting thorough transport assessments, adhering to local parking standards, and engaging with the community. By addressing these criteria, the development can contribute positively to the local transport network and enhance the overall sustainability of the area.

## SA-04 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** What key transport and accessibility criteria must a large residential development site in Bristol satisfy under the local plan and NPPF?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 114, para 115 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

In Bristol, a large residential development site must satisfy several key transport and accessibility criteria as outlined in the Bristol Local Plan – Site Allocations and Development Management Policies (adopted July 2014). The relevant criteria include:

1. **Access to Public Transport**: The development must ensure adequate access to public transport. This includes the provision for public transport improvements where necessary (Extract 1, Extract 5). The aim is to optimize access to sustainable transport modes, which is crucial for reducing reliance on private vehicles.

2. **Transport Improvements**: The development should include appropriate transport improvements to address any unsatisfactory transport conditions that may be created or exacerbated by the development (Extract 1). This is essential to maintain the functionality and safety of the transport network.

3. **Pedestrian and Cyclist Access**: The design and layout of the development should enhance access for pedestrians and cyclists. This includes providing direct, clear, safe, and attractive links to existing routes, local services, amenities, and facilities, including public transport (Extract 7). The integration of walking and cycling networks is vital for promoting sustainable transport options.

4. **Design and Layout Considerations**: The layout, scale, and enclosure of streets and spaces must be appropriate to their function, character, capacity, hierarchy, and local climatic conditions (Extract 7). This ensures that the development is not only functional but also contributes positively to the urban environment.

5. **Linkages with Existing Networks**: New developments are expected to provide links with existing public transport, pedestrian, and cycle networks through their design and layout (Extract 5). This connectivity is crucial for ensuring that the development is well-integrated into the wider transport network.

These criteria align with the principles of sustainable development as outlined in the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF), which emphasizes the importance of promoting sustainable transport and ensuring that developments are accessible to all users. While the specific NPPF paragraphs are not provided in the context, the overarching themes of accessibility and sustainable transport are consistent with NPPF guidance.

In summary, a large residential development in Bristol must prioritize public transport access, implement necessary transport improvements, enhance pedestrian and cyclist access, ensure appropriate design and layout, and provide linkages with existing transport networks to meet local planning requirements effectively.

## SA-05 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** A site adjacent to a protected ancient woodland is proposed for a housing development. Under the NPPF, what are the requirements and what buffer zones apply?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 180c — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

When considering a housing development adjacent to a protected ancient woodland, it is essential to adhere to the guidelines set out in the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) and relevant Planning Practice Guidance (PPG). 

### Key Considerations under the NPPF

1. **Protection of Ancient Woodlands**: The NPPF, particularly in paragraph 180, emphasizes the need to protect and enhance biodiversity and ecosystems. Ancient woodlands are irreplaceable habitats, and any development that may impact them must be carefully assessed. The NPPF states that planning policies and decisions should ensure that development proposals avoid harm to biodiversity and, where possible, enhance it.

2. **Impact Assessment**: Paragraph 180(d) specifically requires that development proposals should be refused if significant harm to biodiversity cannot be avoided, adequately mitigated, or, as a last resort, compensated for. This means that a thorough ecological assessment must be conducted to evaluate the potential impacts of the proposed housing development on the ancient woodland.

3. **Buffer Zones**: While the NPPF does not specify exact buffer zones, it is generally accepted that a buffer zone of at least 15 meters is appropriate to protect ancient woodlands from the impacts of development. This buffer zone helps to mitigate edge effects, such as increased light, noise, and human activity, which can adversely affect the woodland's ecology.

4. **Local Planning Policies**: It is also crucial to review local planning policies, as they may provide specific guidance on buffer zones and the protection of ancient woodlands. Local authorities may have their own policies that align with or expand upon the NPPF's requirements, often reflecting local ecological conditions and community values.

5. **Consultation with Experts**: Engaging with ecological experts and consulting with Natural England is advisable to ensure that the development complies with both national and local policies. They can provide guidance on the necessary assessments and mitigation strategies.

6. **Sustainable Development**: The NPPF promotes sustainable development (paragraph 11), which means that any housing development should not only consider economic and social factors but also environmental impacts. The presumption in favor of sustainable development does not override the need to protect irreplaceable habitats like ancient woodlands.

### Conclusion

In summary, when proposing a housing development adjacent to a protected ancient woodland, it is imperative to conduct a comprehensive ecological assessment, establish an appropriate buffer zone (generally at least 15 meters), and ensure compliance with both the NPPF and local planning policies. The protection of ancient woodlands is a priority, and any development must demonstrate that it will not cause significant harm to these irreplaceable habitats. Engaging with relevant stakeholders and experts will be crucial in navigating this process effectively.

## SA-05 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** medium

**Task:** A site adjacent to a protected ancient woodland is proposed for a housing development. Under the NPPF, what are the requirements and what buffer zones apply?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 180c — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

In considering a housing development adjacent to a protected ancient woodland, the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) provides clear guidance regarding the protection of irreplaceable habitats, such as ancient woodlands. According to Paragraph 175c of the NPPF (2019), development that results in the loss or deterioration of irreplaceable habitats should be refused unless there are wholly exceptional reasons and a suitable compensation strategy exists (Extract 1 and Extract 2). This means that any proposed development must demonstrate that it meets these stringent criteria to be considered acceptable.

Furthermore, the Nottingham Local Plan emphasizes that ancient woodlands and veteran trees are irreplaceable habitats where development will rarely be appropriate (Extract 3 and Extract 4). This reinforces the need for careful consideration and justification for any development proposals in proximity to such sensitive ecological areas.

In addition to the NPPF's requirements, local planning policies may also impose specific buffer zones around ancient woodlands to mitigate potential impacts from development. While the provided context does not specify exact buffer distances, it is common practice in UK planning to establish buffer zones that can range from 15 to 50 meters, depending on the nature of the development and the sensitivity of the woodland. These buffer zones are intended to protect the ecological integrity of the ancient woodland and minimize disturbances from construction activities, noise, and pollution.

Moreover, the Leicester Local Plan highlights the necessity of conducting an Ecological Assessment to identify protected species or priority habitats present on the site and to determine appropriate mitigation measures (Extract 5 and Extract 6). This assessment would be crucial in understanding the potential impacts of the proposed housing development on the adjacent ancient woodland and in formulating a strategy to avoid adverse harm.

In summary, the requirements under the NPPF for a housing development adjacent to a protected ancient woodland include:
1. Refusal of development that leads to loss or deterioration of the ancient woodland unless exceptional reasons and a compensation strategy are provided (NPPF Paragraph 175c).
2. Consideration of local planning policies that may require buffer zones to protect the ancient woodland, typically ranging from 15 to 50 meters.
3. Conducting an Ecological Assessment to identify and mitigate impacts on protected species and habitats.

It is essential for the developer to engage with these requirements thoroughly to ensure compliance with both national and local planning policies.

## SA-06 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** Evaluate the planning merits of a proposed solar farm on agricultural land in the Bristol area. What policies govern renewable energy and what weight is given to loss of agricultural land?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 161, para 174 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (baseline)

When evaluating the planning merits of a proposed solar farm on agricultural land in the Bristol area, it is essential to consider both national and local planning policies that govern renewable energy development, as well as the implications of using agricultural land for such purposes.

### National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF)

The NPPF, particularly in Chapter 14 (Meeting the challenge of climate change, flooding, and coastal change), emphasizes the importance of supporting the transition to a low carbon future. It encourages the development of renewable energy projects, stating that:

- **Paragraph 154**: "The planning system should support the transition to a low carbon future in a changing climate." It highlights the need for local planning authorities to plan for renewable energy developments and to provide a positive strategy for renewable and low carbon energy.

- **Paragraph 155**: "Local planning authorities should approve applications for renewable energy development if its impacts are (or can be made) acceptable." This indicates that the benefits of renewable energy projects should be weighed against their potential impacts.

### Planning Practice Guidance (PPG)

The PPG provides further detail on renewable energy, emphasizing the need for local authorities to consider the following:

- The potential for renewable energy generation in their area.
- The need to balance the benefits of renewable energy against any adverse impacts on the environment, landscape, and local communities.

### Local Planning Policy

In the Bristol area, local planning policies will also play a significant role in the evaluation of the solar farm proposal. The Bristol Local Plan and any relevant supplementary planning documents should be reviewed to identify specific policies related to renewable energy and agricultural land use. 

Typically, local policies may include:

- **Support for Renewable Energy**: Many local plans include policies that support renewable energy projects, particularly in areas identified as suitable for such developments.
- **Protection of Agricultural Land**: Local policies may also include provisions to protect high-quality agricultural land (often classified as Grades 1, 2, and 3a) from development. The National Policy Statement for Renewable Energy Infrastructure (EN-3) states that the loss of agricultural land should be minimized and that the use of lower-grade land should be prioritized.

### Weight Given to Loss of Agricultural Land

The weight given to the loss of agricultural land in the context of a solar farm proposal will depend on several factors:

1. **Grade of Agricultural Land**: If the land is classified as Grade 1 or 2, it is considered to be of high agricultural value, and the loss may be viewed more critically. Conversely, if the land is of lower grade (Grade 3b or below), the impact may be considered less significant.

2. **Local Agricultural Context**: The local context, including the availability of alternative agricultural land and the overall agricultural economy in the area, will influence the weight given to the loss of agricultural land.

3. **Benefits of Renewable Energy**: The contribution of the solar farm to local and national renewable energy targets, carbon reduction goals, and energy security will also be significant. The benefits of reducing greenhouse gas emissions and promoting sustainable energy sources may outweigh the concerns regarding agricultural land loss, particularly if the land is not of the highest quality.

4. **Mitigation Measures**: Proposals that include measures to mitigate the impact on agricultural land, such as dual-use systems (where agriculture can continue alongside solar generation), may be viewed more favorably.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the planning merits of the proposed solar farm on agricultural land in the Bristol area will hinge on a careful assessment of national and local policies regarding renewable energy and agricultural land use. The NPPF and PPG provide a supportive framework for renewable energy, but the protection of high-quality agricultural land remains a significant consideration. A thorough evaluation of the land's agricultural classification, local context, and the potential benefits of the solar farm will be essential in determining the overall planning merits of the proposal. Engaging with local stakeholders and addressing any concerns early in the process will also be crucial for a successful application.

## SA-06 — RAG

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** Evaluate the planning merits of a proposed solar farm on agricultural land in the Bristol area. What policies govern renewable energy and what weight is given to loss of agricultural land?

**Expected policies (check these in the source PDF):** para 161, para 174 — source: `NPPF_December_2024.pdf`

### Output (rag)

To evaluate the planning merits of a proposed solar farm on agricultural land in the Bristol area, we must consider the relevant policies regarding renewable energy and the implications of using agricultural land for such developments.

### Renewable Energy Policies

The context provided does not include specific policies from Bristol regarding renewable energy projects such as solar farms. However, it does highlight the importance of a mix of uses in residential developments, indicating that proposals may be refused if they do not consider the existing mix of uses and their impact on the character, amenity, and economy of the area (Extract 1). This principle may extend to renewable energy projects, suggesting that any proposal for a solar farm would need to demonstrate how it fits within the local context and contributes positively to the area.

### Loss of Agricultural Land

While the context does not explicitly address the loss of agricultural land for renewable energy projects, it is a significant consideration in UK planning policy. The National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) emphasizes the importance of protecting the best and most versatile agricultural land (NPPF paragraph 170). Development on such land should only be permitted if there are no suitable alternatives on lower-quality land, and if the benefits of the development outweigh the loss of agricultural use.

### Evaluation of the Proposed Solar Farm

1. **Impact on Agricultural Land**: If the proposed solar farm is to be located on high-quality agricultural land, it would face significant scrutiny under the NPPF. The applicant would need to provide compelling evidence that the benefits of the solar farm (e.g., renewable energy generation, reduction in carbon emissions) outweigh the loss of agricultural productivity.

2. **Local Context and Mix of Uses**: The proposal must also consider the existing mix of uses in the area. If the solar farm would disrupt the character or amenity of the surrounding area, or if it would negatively impact the local economy, it may be refused planning permission (Extract 1).

3. **Environmental Considerations**: Although not directly mentioned in the provided context, it is essential to consider potential adverse impacts on ecology, biodiversity, and residential amenity, as highlighted in other local plans (e.g., Leicester Local Plan, Extract 7). The proposal would need to demonstrate that it does not adversely affect local wildlife or the quality of life for nearby residents.

### Conclusion

In summary, while the context does not provide specific Bristol policies on solar farms, the principles of protecting agricultural land and ensuring a suitable mix of uses are critical. The proposed solar farm would need to justify its location on agricultural land, demonstrate its benefits, and mitigate any potential negative impacts on the local environment and community. Without addressing these factors, the proposal may struggle to gain planning approval.

## SA-07 — BASELINE

**City:** bristol | **Category:** site_appraisal | **Difficulty:** hard

**Task:** A former NHS hospital site in Bristol is being considered for redevelopment as a mixed-use scheme including housing, offices, and community facilities. What sequential and impact tests apply?

### Output (baseline)

In considering the redevelopment of a former NHS hospital site in Bristol for a mixed-use scheme, including housing, offices, and community facilities, it is essential to apply the relevant sequential and impact tests as outlined in UK planning policy, particularly the National Planning Policy Framework (NPPF) and associated Planning Practice Guidance (PPG).

### Sequential Test

The sequential test is primarily relevant for assessing the location of new development, particularly for retail, leisure, and other town centre uses. According to the NPPF (paragraph 86), the sequential test requires that:

1. **Preference for Town Centre Locations**: Local planning authorities should apply a sequential test to planning applications for main town centre uses (which may include offices and community facilities) to ensure that they are located in town centres first, followed by edge-of-centre locations, and only then in out-of-centre locations where suitable sites are not available.

2. **Assessment of Availability**: The applicant must demonstrate that there are no suitable sites available in the preferred locations. This involves a thorough assessment of existing town centre sites and edge-of-centre sites that could accommodate the proposed development.

3. **Justification for Out-of-Centre Location**: If the site is deemed out-of-centre, the applicant must provide a robust justification for why the development cannot be accommodated in a more sequentially preferable location.

### Impact Test

The impact test is particularly relevant for assessing the potential effects of the proposed development on existing town centres. According to the NPPF (paragraph 89), an impact assessment is required for developments that are not in accordance with an up-to-date local plan and that are likely to have a significant adverse impact on:

1. **Existing Town Centre Vitality and Viability**: The applicant must assess the potential impact of the proposed development on the vitality and viability of existing town centres, particularly in terms of trade diversion from existing businesses.

2. **Local Economic Impact**: The assessment should consider the potential economic impacts on local businesses and the overall economic health of the area.

3. **Scale of Development**: The NPPF suggests that the need for an impact assessment is particularly relevant for larger developments, typically those over a certain threshold (often 2,500 square metres of gross floor space for retail and leisure uses).

### Local Planning Policy

In addition to the NPPF, local planning policies in Bristol will also apply. The Bristol Local Plan may have specific policies regarding the redevelopment of former hospital sites, mixed-use developments, and the provision of community facilities. It is essential to review these local policies to ensure compliance and to understand any additional requirements or considerations that may apply to the site.

### Conclusion

In summary, for the redevelopment of the former NHS hospital site in Bristol, the applicant must conduct a sequential test to demonstrate that there are no suitable town centre or edge-of-centre sites available for the proposed uses. Additionally, an impact assessment will be necessary to evaluate the potential effects on the vitality and viability of existing town centres, particularly if the development is likely to exceed the relevant thresholds set out in the NPPF. Engaging with local planning policies will further ensure that the proposal aligns with both national and local planning objectives.
